قوله : (ومن بعث إلى امرأته شيئاً فقالت هو هدية وقال هو من المهر فالقول قوله في غير المهيأ للأكل) لأنه المملك فكان أعرف بجهة التمليك كيف وإن الظاهر أنه يسعى في إسقاط الواجب إلا فيما يتعارف هدية وهو المهيأ للأكل لأنه متناقض عرفاً. وفسر الإمام الولوالجي المهيأ للأكل بما لا يبقى ويفسد فخرج نحو التمر والدقيق والعسل فإن القول فيه قوله اهـ. ودخل تحت غير المهيأ للأكل الثياب مطلقاً فالقول فيها قوله. وقال الفقية أبو الليث : المختار أن ما كان من متاع سوى ما يجب عليه فالقول له وإلا فلها كالدرع والخمار ومتاع البيت لأن الظاهر يكذبه، والخف والملاءة لا تجب عليه لأنه ليس عليه أن لها أمر خروجها. كذا في غاية البيان. وفي فتح القدير : ثم كون الظاهر يكذبه في نحو الدرع يهيء النكاح مما يثبت بالتسامع فيدعي ورثة ورثة الورثة على ورثة ورثة الورثة ثم وثم فيفضي إلى ذلك اهـ . وفي شرح الجامع للقاضي : فعلى هذا لو كان العهد قريباً ولم يكن متقادماً لا يعجز عن القضاء بمهر المثل فيقضي به قوله ولا يخفى أن محله الخ) قال في الشرنبلالية : فيه تأمل لأنه لا يتأتى ما قاله في حال موتهما اهـ. فلو قال فيما إذا ادعى الزوج أو ورثته لكان أولى. بعد قوله (وهذا يخالف ما ذكره المشايخ (سابقاً) قال الرملي : ليس مخالفاً إذ هو مقيد كما ذكره المشايخ بما قبل التسليم فأي مخالفة ومثله ما في الخلاصة تأمل. ثم رأيت في النهر أقول : لا مخالفة يكون هذا المطلق محمولاً على المقيد وهو عين ما قلته والله تعالى هو الموفق قوله (إنما ينفي احتسابه من المهر الخ) أي لو ادعاه أنه من المهر لا يصدق، أما لو ادعاه من الكسوة الواجية وادعت أنه هدية فإنه يصدق لأن الظاهر لا يكذبه في ذلك بل الظاهر يصدقه فيه وهذا ما سينقله المؤلف عن البحر الرائق ج٣/ ٢١٥
His statement: (And whoever sends something to his wife, and she says it is a gift and he says it is from the mahr, the word is his regarding what is not prepared for eating) because he is the owner, so he is more knowledgeable about the aspect of ownership. Moreover, the apparent is that he seeks to drop the obligatory, except in what is customarily a gift, which is that which is prepared for eating, because it is contradictory customarily. Imam al-Walwaliji interpreted 'prepared for eating' as what does not remain and spoils, so dates, flour, and honey are excluded, for the word is his concerning them. Included under what is not prepared for eating are clothes absolutely, so the word is his concerning them. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: the chosen view is that what is of goods other than what is obligatory for him, the word is his; otherwise it is for her, like the armor and veil and household goods, because the apparent contradicts him. The boot and cloak are not obligatory for him because it is not upon him that she have the matter of her going out. Thus in Ghayat al-Bayan. And in Fath al-Qadir: then the fact that the apparent contradicts him concerning like the armor, marriage is established by hearsay, so the heirs of heirs of heirs claim against the heirs of heirs of heirs, then and then, leading to that. In Sharh al-Jami' lil-Qadi: on this basis, if the time was recent and not remote, he is not unable to judge mahr al-mithl, so he judges accordingly. His statement: 'and it is not hidden that its place is...' He said in al-Shurunbulaliyyah: there is consideration because what he said in the case of their death is not feasible. So if he said when the husband or his heirs claim, it would be better. After his statement: 'This contradicts what the teachers mentioned previously.' Al-Ramli said: it is not contradictory because it is restricted as the teachers mentioned to before surrender, so what contradiction? Similar to what is in al-Khulasah: consideration. Then I saw in al-Nahr: I say: no contradiction; this absolute is carried on the restricted, and that is exactly what I said. God Almighty is the Grantor of success. His statement: (It only negates its being counted from the mahr...) meaning if he claims it is from the mahr, he is not believed. But if he claims it is from the obligatory clothing and she claims it is a gift, he is believed because the apparent does not contradict him in that; rather the apparent confirms him in it. This is what the author will later quote from al-Bahr al-Raiq vol. 3, p. 215.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage addresses a common marital dispute: the husband sends something to his wife, and they differ on whether it was part of the mahr payment or a gift. The ruling is that the husband's word is taken for items that are not customarily given as gifts—specifically, non-perishable non-food items like clothes. For food items that are typically given as gifts (like dates, flour, honey that spoil quickly), the wife's word is taken due to custom (urf). The reasoning is that the husband is the owner and knows his intention, and the apparent custom indicates he wants to satisfy his obligation, but for perishable food, custom suggests it is a gift. Scholars like al-Walwaliji define 'prepared for eating' as what does not last and spoils. Abu al-Layth adds nuance: items that the husband is not obligated to provide (like armor or household goods) are more likely gifts, while obligatory items like clothing may be considered mahr. The text also discusses a procedural point: if the time is recent, mahr al-mithl can be adjudged, but if remote, it becomes difficult. Later, a note clarifies that if the husband claims the item is from obligatory clothing (al-kiswa al-wajiba) and the wife says it's a gift, he is believed because the custom does not contradict him, unlike the mahr claim. This passage relies on custom (urf) and the concept of 'apparent evidence' (zahir) to resolve disputes.

Key Terms
مهيأ للأكل (muhayya' li'l-akl): Items prepared for eating, i.e., perishable food items that customarily are given as gifts.
هدية (hadiyah): A gift given voluntarily, not as part of an obligation like mahr.
مهر (mahr): The obligatory bridal gift that the husband must pay.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, when a husband sends his wife a bag of flour and she claims it is a gift while he claims it is part of the mahr, whose word is taken?
A) The wife's word, because flour is prepared for eating.
B) The husband's word, because flour is not a common gift.
C) The judge decides based on the quantity.
D) The words of both are rejected; the item is split.

Key Takeaway
Custom determines the evidentiary rule: the husband's word for non-food, the wife's word for perishable food gifts.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
8 min
Dispute Over Sent Items: Mahr or Gift
Explaining the ruling on whose word is accepted when husband sends items to wife

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on whose word is accepted in a dispute between spouses over sent items and apply the distinction between edible and non-edible items.

Core Idea
Husband's word for non-edibles; wife's word for edibles due to custom.

When a husband sends something to his wife during marriage, a dispute may arise: he claims it was part of the mahr (dower), she claims it was a gift. Whose statement prevails? The Hanafi school draws a careful distinction based on the nature of the item and customary practice. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute scenario
The husband sends an item to his wife. He later says it was from the mahr, she says it was a gift. No explicit designation was made at the time of sending.
This is a common real-life scenario in marriage.
2
State the Hanafi ruling
The husband's statement is accepted for non-edible items; the wife's statement is accepted for edible items (al-muhayya' li al-akl).
3
Understand the basis: ownership and custom
The husband is the owner (malik) of the wealth, so he knows best the purpose of the transfer. Custom (urf) treats edible items as gifts, so for them the presumption flips to the wife.
This reasoning is based on istihsan (juristic preference) supported by 'urf.
4
Apply the distinction
Examples: clothing (non-edible) → husband's word; fresh fruit (edible) → wife's word. Dates, flour, honey are excluded from 'edible' because they are non-perishable and often considered mahr.
Imam al-Walwaliji defines 'prepared for eating' as perishable items.
5
Note intra-Hanafi nuances and fatwa status
Faqih Abu al-Layth added a nuance for items not obligatory on the husband (e.g., head covering). The fatwa is on the opinion of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn).
Fatawa Qadikhan confirms the mufta bihi position.

فالقول له في غير المهيأ للأكل ولو نكح ذمي ذمية بميته أو بغير مهر وذا جائز عندهم الإمام خلافه كما علمت ولذا قال في فتح القدير: إن تعليل الهداية أو جه، وفي فتاوى قاضيخان الفتوى على قولهما وفي المحيط قال مشايخنا : هذا كله إذا لم تسلم المرأة نفسها، فإن سلمت نفسها ثم وقع الإختلاف في حيال الحياة أو بعد الممات فإنه لا يحكم بمهر المثل لأنا نعلم أن المرأة لا تسلم نفسها من غير أن تتعجل من مهرها شيئاً عادة فيقال لها لا بد أن تقري بما تعجلت وإلا قضينا عليك بالمتعارف، ثم يعمل في الباقي كما ذكرنا اهـ. وأقره عليه الشارحون، ولا يخفى أن محله فيما إذا ادعى الزوج إيصال شيء إليها، أما لو لم يدع فلا ذلك. وفي المحيط معزياً إلى النوادر وامرأة ادعت على زوجها بعد موته أن لها عليه ألف درهم من مهرها فالقول قولها إلى تمام مهر مثلها عند أبي حنيفة لأن مهر المثل يشهد لها اهـ. وهذا يخالف ما ذكره المشايخ سابقاً. وفي الخلاصة من الفصل الثاني عشر من كتاب الدعوى : امرأة ادعت على وارث زوجها مهرها فأنكر الوارث يوقف قدر مهر مثلها ويقول له القاضي أكان مهر مثلها كذا أعلى ذلك؟ إن قالوا لا قال : أكان كذا دون ما قال في المرة من الأولى إلى أن ينتهي إلى مقدار مهر مثلها اهـ.
قوله : (ومن بعث إلى امرأته شيئاً فقالت هو هدية وقال هو من المهر فالقول قوله في غير المهيأ للأكل) لأنه المملك فكان أعرف بجهة التمليك كيف وإن الظاهر أنه يسعى في إسقاط الواجب إلا فيما يتعارف هدية وهو المهيأ للأكل لأنه متناقض عرفاً. وفسر الإمام الولوالجي المهيأ للأكل بما لا يبقى ويفسد فخرج نحو التمر والدقيق والعسل فإن القول فيه قوله اهـ. ودخل تحت غير المهيأ للأكل الثياب مطلقاً فالقول فيها قوله. وقال الفقية أبو الليث : المختار أن ما كان من متاع سوى ما يجب عليه فالقول له وإلا فلها كالدرع والخمار ومتاع البيت لأن الظاهر يكذبه، والخف والملاءة لا تجب عليه لأنه ليس عليه أن لها أمر خروجها. كذا في غاية البيان. وفي فتح القدير : ثم كون الظاهر يكذبه في نحو الدرع النكاح مما يثبت بالتسامع فيدعي ورثة ورثة الورثة على ورثة ورثة الورثة ثم وثم فيفضي إلى ذلك اهـ . وفي شرح الجامع للقاضي : فعلى هذا لو كان العهد لو كان العهد قريباً ولم يكن متقادماً لا يعجز عن القضاء بمهر المثل فيقضي به قوله ولا يخفى أن محله الخ) قال في الشرنبلالية : فيه تأمل لأنه لا يتأتى ما قاله في حال موتهما اهـ. فلو قال فيما إذا ادعى الزوج أو ورثته لكان أولى.

كتاب النكاح باب المهر
والخمار إنما ينفي احتسابه من المهر لا من شيء آخر كالكسوة اهـ . وهذا البحث موافق لما في الجامع الصغير فإنه قال : إلا في الطعام الذي يؤكل فإنه أعم من المهيأ للأكل وغيره. وفيه أيضاً : والذي يجب اعتباره في ديارنا أن جميع ما ذكر من الحنطة واللوز والدقيق والسكر والشاة الحية وباقيها يكون القول فيها قول المرأة لأن المتعارف في ذلك كله أن يرسله هدية، والظاهر مع المرأة لا معه ولا يكون القول له إلا في نحو الثياب والجارية. وهذا كله إذا لم يذكر وقت الدفع جهة أخرى غير المهر فإن ذكر وقال اصرفوا بعض الدنانير إلى الشمع وبعضها إلى الحناء لا يقبل قوله بعد ذلك أنه من المهر كما في القنية. وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو بعث إليها ثوباً وقال هو من الكسوة وقالت هدية فإن القول قوله والبينة بينتها. كذا في الخلاصة من كتاب الدعوى. وهذا يدل على أن البينة بينتها في مسألة الكتاب أيضاً لعدم الفرق بينهما. وأراد بكون القول قوله في المختصر أن يحلف فإن حلف إن كان المتاع قائماً كان للمرأة أن ترد المتاع لأنها لم ترض بكونه مهراً وترجع على الزوج بما بقي من المهر، وإن كان المتاع هالكاً إن كان شيئاً مثلياً ردت على الزوج مثل ذلك، وإن لم يكن مثلياً لا ترجع على الزوج بما بقي من المهر. كذا في الخانية وهذا إذا لم يكن من جنس المهر، فإن كان من جنسه وقع قصاصاً كما لا يخفى. وصرح في معراج الدراية أن فيما كان القول فيه قولها وهو المهيا للأكل فإنه مع يمينها وإن كان العرف شاهداً لها . وأشار المصنف إلى أن الزوج لو بعث إليها هدايا وعوضته المرأة ثم زفت إليه ثم فارقها وسال بعثتها إليك عارية وأراد أن يسترده وأرادت هي أن تسترد العوض فالقول قوله في لأنه أنكر التمليك، وإذا استرده تسترد هي ما عوضته. كذا في الفتاوى السمرقندية . انتيح القدير : ولو بعث هو وبعث أبوها له أيضاً ثم قال هو من المهر فللأب أن يرجع في الخلاصة قوله (وهذا البحث موافق لما في الجامع الصغير) كذا في النسخ وقعت هذه الجملة قبل قوله وفيه أيضاً» أي في الفتح والذي ينبغي ذكرها بعده تأمل قوله (بما يقي من المهر) أي إن كان دفع لها شيئاً منه قوله (وإن كان المتاع هالكاً قال في النهر : وفي البزازية اتخذ لها ثياباً ولبستها حتى تخرقت ثم قال هو من المهر وقالت هو من النفقة أعني الكسوة فالقول لها قيل فما الفرق بينه وبين ما إذا كان الثوب قائماً حيث يكون القول ثمة له؟ قلنا : الفرق أن في القائم اتفقا على أصل التمليك واختلفا في صفته والقول للمالك لأنه أعرف بجهة التمليك بخلاف الهالك لأنه يدعي سقوط بعض المهر والمرأة تنكر ذلك. فإن قيل : لم لم يجعل هذا اختلافاً في جهة التمليك كالقائم؟ قلنا بالهلاك خرج عن المملوكية والاختلاف في أصل التمليك أو في جهته ولا ملك بحال باطل فيكون اختلافاً في ضمان الهالك وبدله فالقول لمن يملك البدل والضمان .اهـ. وهذا يقتضي أن القول لها فيما لو كان هالكاً في مسألة الكتاب لأنه بذلك يدعي عليها الهالك وهي تنكر وهو مخالف لما قدمنا والفرق يعسر فتدبره قوله (وإن لم يكن مثلياً لا ترجع الخ) أي لأنه تجب قيمته مقوماً بالدراهم وهي من جنس
So the statement [of the husband] is accepted regarding what is not prepared for eating. And if a dhimmi man marries a dhimmi woman with carrion or without a dower, that is permissible according to them, but the Imam [Abu Hanifa] opposed as you know. Hence it is said in Fath al-Qadir that the explanation of al-Hidayah is objectionable. And in the Fatawa of Qadikhan, the fatwa is on their statement [Abu Yusuf and Muhammad]. In al-Muhit, our mashayikh said: 'All this is when the woman has not surrendered herself. If she surrendered herself and then a dispute occurs during life or after death, then mahr al-mithl is not judged, because we know that a woman does not surrender herself without having received something of her mahr in advance as a custom. So she is told: you must acknowledge what you received, otherwise we will decree against you the customary [mahr]. Then the remainder is dealt with as we mentioned.' The commentators concurred with it. It is not hidden that its place is when the husband claims delivering something to her. But if he does not claim, then not. In al-Muhit, attributed to al-Nawadir: 'A woman claimed against her husband after his death that he owed her one thousand dirhams from her mahr. Her statement is accepted up to the full amount of her mahr al-mithl according to Abu Hanifa, because mahr al-mithl testifies for her.' This contradicts what the mashayikh mentioned earlier. In al-Khulasa, from the twelfth chapter of the Book of Claims: 'A woman claimed against the heir of her husband her mahr and the heir denied. The judge withholds the amount of her mahr al-mithl and says to the heir: "Was her mahr al-mithl such? Higher than that?" If he says no, he says: "Was it such? Lower than what he said in the first instance?" until it reaches the amount of her mahr al-mithl.'
His statement: (And whoever sends to his wife something and she says it is a gift and he says it is from the mahr, then the statement is his regarding what is not prepared for eating) because he is the owner, so he is more knowledgeable of the purpose of ownership. Moreover, the apparent [custom] is that he seeks to drop an obligation, except for what is customarily a gift, which is what is prepared for eating, because that is contradictory to custom. Imam al-Walwaliji interpreted 'prepared for eating' as what does not remain and spoils. So dates, flour, and honey are excluded; the statement [of the husband] is accepted about them. Under 'not prepared for eating' are included clothing absolutely, so the husband's statement is accepted about them. Faqih Abu al-Layth said: 'The chosen view is that what is of goods other than what is obligatory on him, his statement is accepted; otherwise it is for her, like a shift (dir'), a head covering (khimar), and household goods, because the apparent [custom] belies him. But a light boot (khuff) and a wrap (mala'a) are not obligatory on him, because he is not obligated to provide for her going out.' As in Ghayat al-Bayan. In Fath al-Qadir: 'Then that the apparent belies him regarding something like a shift (dir')... marriage is established by hearsay, so the heirs of the heirs of the heirs claim against the heirs of the heirs of the heirs, and so on, leading to that.' In Sharh al-Jami' by al-Qadi: 'On this basis, if the covenant was recent and not remote, he is not incapable of judging mahr al-mithl, so he judges it.' His statement: 'It is not hidden that its place...' He said in al-Shurnabulaliyya: 'There is reflection in it, because what he said cannot occur when both are dead.' So it would be better if he said: 'when the husband or his heirs claim'.
After his statement: (This contradicts what the mashayikh mentioned earlier). Al-Ramli said: 'It is not contradictory, because it is restricted as the mashayikh mentioned to before surrender. So what contradiction? And similarly what is in al-Khulasa is to be pondered.' Then I saw in al-Nahr: 'I say: there is no contradiction; this absolute is carried upon the restricted, which is exactly what I said. And Allah is the Grantor of success.'
His statement: (He only denies its counting from the mahr...) i.e., if he claims it is from the mahr, he is not believed. But if he claims it is from the obligatory clothing (kiswa) and she claims it is a gift, then he is believed, because the apparent does not belie him in that; rather the apparent confirms him. And this is what the author will quote from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq 3/215.
Book of Nikah, Chapter of Mahr. And the head covering (khimar) only denies its counting from the mahr, not from something else like clothing. This research agrees with what is in al-Jami' al-Saghir, for he said: 'Except for food that is eaten, for it is more general than what is prepared for eating and other than it.' And in it also: 'What must be considered in our lands is that all that is mentioned of wheat, almonds, flour, sugar, a live sheep, and the rest, the statement about them is the woman's, because the custom in all that is that he sends it as a gift, and the apparent is with the woman not with him. His statement is accepted only for something like clothing and a slave girl.' All this is when at the time of payment he did not mention another purpose besides the mahr. If he mentioned and said: 'Spend some of the dinars on wax and some on henna,' his later claim that it is from the mahr is not accepted, as in al-Qunyah. And the author indicated that if he sends her a garment and says it is from the clothing (kiswa) and she says it is a gift, then his statement is accepted, and the burden of proof is on her. As in al-Khulasa from the Book of Claims. This indicates that the burden of proof is on her in the issue of the book as well, because there is no difference between them. And he intended by 'his statement is accepted' in al-Mukhtasar that he takes an oath. If he swears, then if the item is still extant, the woman may return the item because she did not consent to it being mahr, and she claims from the husband the remainder of the mahr. If the item is destroyed, if it is a thing of the same kind (mithli), she returns to the husband its like; if it is not mithli, she does not claim from the husband the remainder of the mahr. As in al-Khaniyya. This is when it is not of the same type as the mahr. If it is of the same type, it constitutes a set-off (qisas), as is not hidden. And it is stated in Mi'raj al-Diraya that in what the woman's statement is accepted, namely what is prepared for eating, it is with her oath, even if custom testifies for her. And the author indicated that if the husband sends gifts to her and she compensates him, then she is married to him, then they separate, and he says: 'I sent them to you as a loan and I want to take them back,' and she wants to take back the compensation, then his statement is accepted because he denies ownership. And if he takes them back, she takes back what she compensated him. As in al-Fatawa al-Samarqandiyya. In Fath al-Qadir: 'And if he sends and her father also sends for him, then he says it is from the mahr, the father has the right to take back...' In al-Khulasa, his statement: (This research agrees with what is in al-Jami' al-Saghir). In the copies, this sentence occurs before his statement: 'And in it also...' i.e., in al-Fath, and it should be mentioned after it. Reflect. His statement: (from the remainder of the mahr) i.e., if he had paid some of it to her. His statement: (And if the item is destroyed). He said in al-Nahr: 'In al-Bazzaziyya: He had clothes made for her and she wore them until they were worn out, then he said they are from the mahr and she said they are from maintenance (kiswa). The statement is hers. It was asked: What is the difference between this and when the garment is extant, where the statement is his? We answer: The difference is that when extant, they agree on the ownership and differ on its description, and the statement is of the owner because he knows better the purpose of ownership. In the case of the destroyed, he claims a reduction of some mahr and she denies that. If it is said: Why is this not made a difference in the purpose of ownership like the extant? We say: By destruction, it left the state of being owned, and the dispute is about the ownership or its purpose, and there is no ownership at all, so it is a dispute about the guarantee of the destroyed and its substitute, so the statement belongs to the one who owns the substitute and the guarantee.' This implies that the statement is hers if it is destroyed in the issue of the book, because by that he claims the destroyed against her while she denies, which contradicts what we presented, and the difference is difficult. So ponder it. His statement: (And if it is not mithli, she does not claim...) i.e., because its value must be paid in dirhams, which are of the same type.
Source pages p. 321

Commentary
This passage is an expanded commentary (sharh) on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding disputes between spouses over items the husband sends to the wife during marriage. The central ruling is: if the husband sends something and the wife says it is a gift while the husband says it is part of the mahr (dower), the husband's word is taken for non-edible items, while the wife's word is taken for edible items. The reason given is that the husband is the owner of the wealth, so he knows best the purpose of the transfer (whether as mahr or gift). Additionally, the apparent custom (al-zahir 'urfan) is that he seeks to fulfill his obligation (mahr) by sending things, so he would not intentionally give gifts that count toward the mahr. However, this presumption is reversed for edible items like food, because custom treats such items as gifts rather than mahr. Imam al-Walwaliji defines 'prepared for eating' as perishable items that do not remain (e.g., fresh dates, flour, honey). For non-edibles like clothing, the husband's word is accepted. Faqih Abu al-Layth adds a nuance: for items that are not obligatory on the husband (like a shift or head covering), the wife's word is taken because custom belies the husband. The text then discusses a related scenario: after the wife has surrendered herself (taslim al-nafs), and a dispute arises about the mahr, the mashayikh say that mahr al-mithl is not awarded because it is assumed she received something in advance. This is contrasted with a narration from al-Nawadir where after the husband's death, the wife's claim of a specific mahr amount is accepted up to mahr al-mithl according to Abu Hanifa, even if she had surrendered. The apparent contradiction is reconciled by al-Ramli and others: the death case is restricted to before surrender (qabl al-taslim), while the mashayikh's ruling applies after surrender. The passage also covers details about the burden of proof when the husband claims the item is for clothing (kiswa) versus mahr, the effect of the item being extant or destroyed on the wife's right to return it and claim the remainder of the mahr, and the rule when the husband gives an explicit direction for the item (e.g., for wax or henna) so his later claim that it was mahr is rejected. The overall ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference) supported by custom ('urf), which is a recognized source of law in the Hanafi school. The fatwa in later works (like Fatawa Qadikhan) is on the opinion of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn).

Key Terms
المهر (al-mahr): The dower or bridal gift that the husband pays to the wife as an obligation of marriage.
المهيأ للأكل (al-muhayya' li al-akl): Items prepared for eating, i.e., foodstuffs that are typically perishable and customarily given as gifts rather than as mahr.
القول قوله (al-qawl qawlu): His statement is accepted (as the default in a dispute); the burden of proof is on the other party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The husband's statement is always accepted.

C) The item is divided equally.
D) The testimony of witnesses is required.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school distinguishes between edible and non-edible items in disputes over whether sent items are mahr or gifts, accepting the husband's word for non-edibles and the wife's word for edibles based on custom and ownership.

Whose Word is Accepted in Disputed Pre-Marriage Gifts
Explains whose word is accepted when husband sends items to wife before marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule for resolving disputes over pre-marriage gifts by identifying which party's word is accepted based on the nature of the item and custom.

Core Idea
Custom determines whose word is accepted: woman's for food gifts, man's for clothes and slaves.

When a husband sends items to his wife before marriage, disputes may arise about whether those items were part of the mahr (dower) or separate gifts. The Hanafi school relies on custom (urf) and the default presumption to resolve such disputes. This lesson examines the detailed rules from Bahr al-Ra'iq, expanding on the principles from al-Jami' al-Saghir, and clarifies the procedural steps including oaths and evidentiary burdens.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nature of the item
Classify the sent item into categories: food items (e.g., wheat, almonds, sugar, live sheep) vs. clothes and slave girls. This determines the default presumption.
Food items are customarily sent as gifts; clothes and slaves are customarily part of mahr.
2
Apply the custom-based presumption
For food items, the woman's word is accepted; for clothes and slaves, the husband's word is accepted. This is based on the principle that custom indicates the likely intent.
Custom is a valid legal consideration in Hanafi jurisprudence.
3
Check for explicit statement at time of sending
If the husband explicitly stated that the items are from the mahr at the time of sending, his word is accepted regardless of custom. If he later claims it was mahr without having stated so, his claim is not accepted.
Explicit intention overrides default presumption.
4
Determine whose word is accepted and the oath
The party whose word is accepted must take an oath. For example, if the woman's word is accepted, she swears that it was a gift; if the husband's word, he swears it was mahr.
Oath is a procedural requirement to affirm the claim.
5
Apply consequences based on oath
If the husband takes an oath and the property is extant, the wife may return it and claim the remainder of the mahr. If destroyed, she compensates if it is fungible (mithli) else not. If the item is of same type as mahr, it counts as discharge.
The outcome depends on the status of the property and its type.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخمار إنما ينفي احتسابه من المهر لا من شيء آخر كالكسوة اهـ . وهذا البحث موافق لما في الجامع الصغير فإنه قال : إلا في الطعام الذي يؤكل فإنه أعم من المهيأ للأكل وغيره. وفيه أيضاً : والذي يجب اعتباره في ديارنا أن جميع ما ذكر من الحنطة واللوز والدقيق والسكر والشاة الحية وباقيها يكون القول فيها قول المرأة لأن المتعارف في ذلك كله أن يرسله هدية، والظاهر مع المرأة لا معه ولا يكون القول له إلا في نحو الثياب والجارية. وهذا كله إذا لم يذكر وقت الدفع جهة أخرى غير المهر فإن ذكر وقال اصرفوا بعض الدنانير إلى الشمع وبعضها إلى الحناء لا يقبل قوله بعد ذلك أنه من المهر كما في القنية. وأشار المصنف إلى أنه لو بعث إليها ثوباً وقال هو من الكسوة وقالت هدية فإن القول قوله والبينة بينتها. كذا في الخلاصة من كتاب الدعوى. وهذا يدل على أن البينة بينتها في مسألة الكتاب أيضاً لعدم الفرق بينهما. وأراد بكون القول قوله في المختصر أن يحلف فإن حلف إن كان المتاع قائماً كان للمرأة أن ترد المتاع لأنها لم ترض بكونه مهراً وترجع على الزوج بما بقي من المهر، وإن كان المتاع هالكاً إن كان شيئاً مثلياً ردت على الزوج مثل ذلك، وإن لم يكن مثلياً لا ترجع على الزوج بما بقي من المهر. كذا في الخانية وهذا إذا لم يكن من جنس المهر، فإن كان من جنسه وقع قصاصاً كما لا يخفى. وصرح في معراج الدراية أن فيما كان القول فيه قولها وهو المهيا للأكل فإنه مع يمينها وإن كان العرف شاهداً لها . وأشار المصنف إلى أن الزوج لو بعث إليها هدايا وعوضته المرأة ثم زفت إليه ثم فارقها وسال بعثتها إليك عارية وأراد أن يسترده وأرادت هي أن تسترد العوض فالقول قوله في لأنه أنكر التمليك، وإذا استرده تسترد هي ما عوضته. كذا في الفتاوى السمرقندية .
And the head covering (khimar) only negates its inclusion from the mahr, not from something else like clothing (kiswa). This research agrees with what is in al-Jami' al-Saghir, for he said: except for food that is eaten, for it is more general than what is prepared for eating and other than it. And in it also: And what must be considered in our regions is that all that is mentioned of wheat, almonds, flour, sugar, live sheep, and the rest, the word in them is the woman's, because the custom in all that is that he sends it as a gift, and the apparent circumstance is with the woman, not with him, and his word is not accepted except in the case of clothes and a slave girl. And all this is if he did not mention at the time of payment another aspect other than mahr; if he did mention and said 'spend some of the dinars on wax and some on henna', his word afterwards that it is from the mahr is not accepted, as in al-Qunyah. And the author indicated that if he sent her a garment and said it is from the kiswa, and she said it is a gift, then his word is accepted, and the burden of proof is on her. Thus in al-Khulasah from the Book of Claims. And this indicates that the burden of proof is on her in the issue of the book as well, due to no difference between them. And he intended by 'his word is accepted' in al-Mukhtasar that he should take an oath; if he takes an oath, if the property is extant, the woman may return the property because she did not consent to it being mahr, and she may claim from the husband the remainder of the mahr; and if the property is destroyed, if it is something fungible (mithli), she returns the like of it to the husband; if it is not fungible, she does not claim from the husband the remainder of the mahr. Thus in al-Khaniyah. And this is if it is not of the same type as the mahr; if it is of the same type, it becomes a discharge (qisas) as is not hidden. And it is explicitly stated in Miraj al-Dirayah that in that which his word is accepted (the woman's word) — and it is what is prepared for eating — then it is with her oath, even if custom supports her. And the author indicated that if the husband sent her gifts and the woman compensated him, then she was brought to him, then he separated from her, and she said 'my sending to you was a loan' and he wants to take it back, and she wants to take back the compensation, then his word is accepted because he denies the transfer of ownership; and when he takes it back, she takes back what she compensated him. Thus in al-Fatawa al-Samarqandiyyah.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq is an expansion on the rules of mahr (dower) disputes before marriage. It begins by clarifying a prior point about the khimar (head covering) being considered as mahr only, not as something else like clothing (kiswa). Then it agrees with al-Jami' al-Saghir that for food items that are customarily eaten, the default classification is that they are sent as gifts, not mahr. Therefore, in disputes, the woman's word is accepted for such items. For clothes and slave girls, the man's word is accepted because custom treats these as part of the mahr. However, if the husband explicitly states at the time of sending that the items are from the mahr, his word is accepted; otherwise, his later claim is not accepted. The passage then discusses the scenario where the husband sends a garment calling it 'kiswa' (clothing allowance) and the wife says it is a gift: here, the husband's word is accepted, but the wife must bring evidence (al-bayyinah) to support her claim. This is analogized to the ruling in al-Mukhtasar. If the husband takes an oath and the property is still extant, the wife may return it and claim the remainder of the mahr. If the property is destroyed and it is a fungible thing (mithli), she must return a similar item; if it is non-fungible, she does not have to pay compensation for the remainder of the mahr. If the sent item is of the same type as the agreed mahr, it counts as a discharge (qisas). The passage concludes with a scenario where the husband sends gifts, the wife compensates him, then they separate: the husband's word is accepted that the gifts were a loan (or not a transfer of ownership), so he can take them back, and the woman can take back her compensation. This is from the Fatawa al-Samarqandiyyah.

Key Terms
المهر (al-mahr): The dower or bridal gift that the husband must give to the wife as part of the marriage contract.
الكسوة (al-kiswa): Clothing or apparel provided by the husband as maintenance (nafaqah), distinct from mahr.
القول قوله/قها (al-qawlu qawluhu/qawluha): A legal maxim meaning that one party's word is given preference in a dispute, usually with an oath.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The husband's word is accepted only if he took an oath
D) Neither, it goes to the judge

Key Takeaway
In disputes over pre-marriage gifts, the default presumption based on custom determines whose word is accepted: food → woman's word; clothes/slaves → man's word.

Father's Sending and Right to Take Back
Ruling on father's right to recover items sent when he later claims they were mahr.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when both husband and father send items, and apply the rule that the father has the right to take back if he claims his sending was from the mahr.

Core Idea
When both father and husband send, and the father claims his is from mahr, he has the right to take it back.

This lesson addresses a specific scenario from Fath al-Qadir: if the husband sends something and the wife's father also sends something, and then the father later claims that his sending was part of the mahr, does he have the right to take it back? The text rules that he does. We will explore the reasoning behind this ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify parties and actions
Recognize that both the husband and the wife's father sent items. The father later claims his sending was from the mahr.
The source text from Fath al-Qadir begins with 'If he sent and her father sent to him too, then he said it is from the mahr.'
2
Determine the legal issue
The issue is whether the father can take back his items after claiming they were part of the mahr.
3
State the ruling
The ruling is that the father has the right to take back his items. This is from al-Khulasah and Fath al-Qadir.
The text says 'then the father has the right to take it back.'
4
Consider the basis
The father's claim is accepted because he is the owner, and his statement about the mode of transfer is given weight.
This aligns with the general principle that the owner knows best the mode of transfer.

It was said: What is the difference between this and when the garment is extant where the word is his there? We say: The difference is that in the extant case, they both agree on the original transfer of ownership but differ on its description, and the word is the owner's because he is more knowledgeable about the mode of transfer, unlike the destroyed case because he claims a reduction of part of the mahr and the woman denies that. If it is said: Why is this not considered a difference in the mode of transfer like the extant case? We say: By destruction it leaves the status of being owned, and the dispute is about the original transfer of ownership or its mode, and without any ownership it is invalid, so the dispute becomes about the guarantee of the destroyed item and its substitute, so the word is for the one who owns the substitute and the guarantee. His statement (and if it is not fungible, she does not claim the remainder of the mahr) i.e., because its value is obligatory, valued in dirhams, and it is of the kind of this mahr, so it becomes a discharge, so she does not claim the remainder of the mahr if the value is the amount remaining for her. His statement (and a distinction is made between what preceded and what is here) it can be distinguished that what preceded is depicted when she explicitly stated compensation, unlike here where it is an acknowledgment of the father's action without explicit statement. He said in al-Tatar Khaniyyah: And if he sent to a woman a nafjah of musk or perfume, then said it was from the mahr, the word is his; then if she sent him a compensation for that perfume and thought that her husband sent it as a gift, then when the dispute appeared she wanted to take back the compensation, does she have that? His statement (and if he sent her a cow) He said in al-Nahr: This may be problematic based on what preceded, because he is the owner in this situation, so he is more knowledgeable about the mode of transfer, and therefore al-Qadi said: The word should be the husband's. End. I say: The depiction of the issue according to al-Imadiyyah and al-Tatar Khaniyyah and others: A woman died, so her mother held a mourning ceremony, and the husband of the deceased sent a cow to the woman's mother, and she slaughtered it... to the end here, and by it the answer appears. She is or...
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq continues the discussion of disputes over sent items before marriage. It begins with a quote from Fath al-Qadir: if both the husband and the wife's father send items, and the father later claims his sending was from the mahr, he has the right to take it back. The commentator then discusses a marginal note about the placement of a sentence. Next, it delves into a scenario from al-Bazzaziyyah: if the husband provides clothes that the wife wears until they are torn, then he claims it was mahr and she says it was nafaqah (maintenance), the woman's word is accepted if the clothes are destroyed. The reasoning is that when the item is extant, both parties agree on the transfer of ownership but differ on its classification; the owner (husband) is more knowledgeable about the mode of transfer. When destroyed, the dispute is about whether the husband reduced the mahr, and the woman denies; therefore, she is not liable. If the item is fungible, she must return a like item; if non-fungible, she pays value, which may discharge the mahr. The passage then compares this with the case of the father's sending and the compensation scenario from al-Tatar Khaniyyah: if the husband sends musk and the wife sends compensation thinking it was a gift, she cannot take back the compensation unless she explicitly stated it was compensation at the time of sending (according to Abu Bakr al-Iskaaf). Qadi Khan holds a different view. The commentator tries to reconcile by distinguishing intent. Finally, a case from al-Nahr: a cow sent by the husband to his deceased wife's mother during a mourning ceremony; the mother slaughters it and feeds people. The husband later claims it was from the mahr. The text suggests his word is accepted because he is the owner, but the commentator notes it may be problematic. The answer is sought from other works.

Key Terms
بعث (ba'th): Sending items by the husband or father
مهر (mahr): Dower or bride-price given by the husband to the wife

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the passage, when does the father have the right to take back his sent items?
A) Whenever he changes his mind
B) If he claims his sending was from the mahr and both he and the husband sent items
C) Only if the husband agrees
D) Only if the items are still unused

Key Takeaway
The father who sends items and later claims they were from the mahr has the right to take them back if both parties sent items.

Husband's Clothes: Extant vs Destroyed
Distinction in dispute resolution based on whether the gifted property is extant or destroyed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the ruling on whose word is accepted when the husband claims clothes were mahr and the wife claims they were nafaqah, based on whether the clothes are extant or destroyed.

Core Idea
If clothes are extant, husband's word is accepted; if destroyed, wife's word is accepted.

This lesson examines a scenario from al-Bazzaziyyah: the husband provides clothes to his wife, which she wears until they are torn. Later, he claims they were mahr, and she says they were nafaqah (maintenance). The ruling depends on whether the clothes still exist or have been destroyed. We will explore the reasoning behind this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
The husband says the clothes he gave were part of the mahr; the wife says they were for maintenance (nafaqah).
The clothes are the subject of the dispute.
2
Determine the state of the clothes
If the clothes are still extant (qā'im), the husband's word is accepted. If they are destroyed (hālik), the wife's word is accepted.
The passage explicitly states this distinction.
3
Understand the reasoning for extant
When the clothes are extant, both agree on the transfer of ownership but differ on its classification (mahr vs nafaqah). The owner (husband) knows best the mode of transfer.
The principle: 'al-qawlu li al-mālik' (the word is the owner's).
4
Understand the reasoning for destroyed
When destroyed, the dispute is about whether the husband reduced the mahr (by claiming the clothes were mahr) and the wife denies this. The wife is not liable for a claim of reduction, so her word is accepted.
The passage explains that the destruction changes the nature of the dispute.
5
Consider the exception for non-fungible items
If the destroyed item is non-fungible (not mithlī), the wife must pay its value, which may discharge part of the mahr.
This is a nuance that affects the final ruling.

Woman's Compensation: Explicit Statement Required?
Dispute over the requirement of explicit statement for recovery of compensaton.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the two Hanafi views on whether a woman who sent compensation for gifts she thought were from her husband can recover it if the husband later claims the gifts were mahr, based on whether she explicitly stated it was compensation.

Core Idea
The woman can take back compensation only if she explicitly stated it was compensation; otherwise, it is a new gift.

This lesson deals with a complex scenario: a husband sends a valuable item (like musk) to his wife, who then sends him a compensation thinking it was a gift. Later, the husband claims the item was actually mahr. The question is: can the wife take back her compensation? There are two views: one (Qadi Khan) says yes, she can take it back because the basis for compensation (a gift) is absent; another (Abu Bakr al-Iskaaf) says no, unless she explicitly stated at the time that it was compensation. We will analyze the dispute and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Set the scenario
The husband sends a musk or perfume to his wife. The wife, thinking it is a gift, sends back a compensation (e.g., money). Later, the husband says the musk was mahr, not a gift.
The wife intended to reciprocate the gesture, not pay mahr.
2
Identify the legal issue
Can the wife recover the compensation she sent? The issue is whether her compensation was valid and whether she can reverse it.
3
State the two Hanafi views
Qadi Khan: She can take it back because she sent it as compensation for a gift, and if it wasn't a gift, the compensation is void. Abu Bakr al-Iskaaf: She can only take it back if she explicitly stated at the time that it was compensation.
The text presents both views without a definitive choice.
4
Analyze the reasoning
Qadi Khan's view: The compensation is contingent on the item being a gift; if the husband denies it, the basis disappears. Al-Iskaaf's view: Without explicit statement, the compensation is an independent gift.
The distinction is about the woman's intention: was it clearly compensation or just a gift?
5
Note the tarjih
The author of Bahr al-Ra'iq seems to lean towards Qadi Khan's view, as he says 'the apparent meaning of the author's words is that explicit statement is not a condition.'
But the issue remains open to interpretation.

Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq continues the discussion of disputes over sent items before marriage. It begins with a quote from Fath al-Qadir: if both the husband and the wife's father send items, and the father later claims his sending was from the mahr, he has the right to take it back. The commentator then discusses a marginal note about the placement of a sentence. Next, it delves into a scenario from al-Bazzaziyyah: if the husband provides clothes that the wife wears until they are torn, then he claims it was mahr and she says it was nafaqah (maintenance), the woman's word is accepted if the clothes are destroyed. The reasoning is that when the item is extant, both parties agree on the transfer of ownership but differ on its classification; the owner (husband) is more knowledgeable about the mode of transfer. When destroyed, the dispute is about whether the husband reduced the mahr, and the woman denies; therefore, she is not liable. If the item is fungible, she must return a like item; if non-fungible, she pays value, which may discharge the mahr. The passage then compares this with the case of the father's sending and the compensation scenario from al-Tatar Khaniyyah: if the husband sends musk and the wife sends compensation thinking it was a gift, she cannot take back the compensation unless she explicitly stated it was compensation at the time of sending (according to Abu Bakr al-Iskaaf). Qadi Khan holds a different view. The commentator tries to reconcile by distinguishing intent. Finally, a case from al-Nahr: a cow sent by the husband to his deceased wife's mother during a mourning ceremony; the mother slaughters it and feeds people. The husband later claims it was from the mahr. The text suggests his word is accepted because he is the owner, but the commentator notes it may be problematic. The answer is sought from other works.

Key Terms
تعويض (ta'wid): Compensation given for a gift
تصريح (tasrīh): Explicit statement of intention

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the practical difference between Qadi Khan's view and Abu Bakr al-Iskaaf's view?
A) Qadi Khan requires explicit statement; al-Iskaaf does not
B) Al-Iskaaf requires explicit statement; Qadi Khan does not
C) Both require explicit statement
D) Neither requires explicit statement

Key Takeaway
The woman's ability to recover compensation for a gift that turned out to be mahr depends on whether she explicitly stated it was compensation; there are two Hanafi views on this.

Cow Scenario: Husband's Sending to Mother-in-Law
Dispute over nature of a cow sent during mourning: gift or mahr payment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on whose word is accepted when a husband sends a cow to his deceased wife's mother for a mourning ceremony, and later claims it was from the mahr, and identify the potential problem with this ruling.

Core Idea
When the husband sends a cow after his wife's death, his word is generally accepted because he is the owner, but the context of mourning may affect the ruling.

This final lesson addresses a specific scenario: a woman dies, her mother holds a mourning ceremony, and the husband sends a cow to the mother, who slaughters it and feeds the people. Later, the husband claims the cow was part of the mahr (outstanding dower). The ruling from al-Nahr says the husband's word is accepted because he is the owner and more knowledgeable about the mode of transfer. However, the commentator notes a potential problem, which we will explore.

Guided Path
1
Understand the scenario
A wife dies. Her mother holds a mourning ceremony (ma'tam). The husband sends a cow to the mother, who slaughters it and feeds the attendees. Later, the husband says the cow was from the mahr.
The cow is consumed in the process.
2
Identify the legal issue
Is the cow considered a gift or payment of mahr? Whose word is accepted?
3
State the apparent ruling
According to al-Nahr and others, the husband's word is accepted because he is the owner and knows best the mode of transfer.
This follows the general rule for extant property.
4
Note the potential problem
The commentator (in Bahr al-Ra'iq) says this may be problematic because the husband is the one making the claim, and the context (mourning, feeding) might indicate it was a gift.
The passage suggests the answer may be found in other works.
5
Seek resolution
The commentator refers to al-Imadiyyah and al-Tatar Khaniyyah for the correct depiction. The answer is not fully given in this excerpt.
The text ends abruptly.

Wife's Right to Reclaim Compensation Sent to Husband
Explains conditions for wife to reclaim compensation sent to husband.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on when a wife can reclaim compensation sent to her husband, distinguishing based on whether the husband admitted ownership of the item.

Core Idea
A wife's compensation is invalid if the husband denies that the item was a gift, so she can reclaim it.

This passage from Ibn Nujaym's al-Bahr al-Rā'iq delves into a nuanced situation: a wife sends compensation (taʿwīḍ) to her husband, believing he gave her an item as a gift. When the husband later claims the item was part of her mahr (dower), the question arises whether she can reclaim the compensation. The author distinguishes based on whether the husband admitted ownership of the item, and also addresses related scenarios like disputes over the nature of payments and a cow sent at a family death.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the dispute
Recognize that the wife sent compensation (taʿwīḍ) under the assumption that her husband owned the item (i.e., it was a gift). The husband denies ownership, claiming the item was part of the mahr. The key issue is whether her compensation was valid.
2
Determine the basis of compensation
The compensation's validity depends on whether the husband actually owned the item and thus could gift it. If he denies ownership, the basis (tamleek) fails, making the compensation invalid.
3
Apply the rule: invalid compensation allows reclaim
Since the husband's denial shows the wife's assumption was wrong, the compensation is not a valid gift (hibah) from her. Hence she can reclaim it. If the husband had admitted the gift, the compensation would be valid and she could not reclaim.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
In a parallel case of a cow sent by the husband, Qādī Khān holds that the husband's statement (denying gift) is accepted by default, while the apparent position (masʼalah) favors the wife's statement. Understand the practical difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويفرق بين ما سبق أن في الأول التعويض منها كان بناء على ظنها التمليك منه وقد أنكره فلم يصح التعويض فلم يكن هبة منها فلها الاسترداد وفي الثانية حصل التمليك فصح التعويض فلا رجوع لها، وقد يقال التعويض على ظن الهبة لا مطلقاً وقد أنكرها فينبغي أن ترجع . وقيد المصنف بكونه ادعاه مهراً لأنها لو ادعت أنه من المهر وقال هو وديعة فإن كان من جنس المهر فالقول قولها، وإن كان من خلافه فالقول قوله. وأطلق في البعث فشمل ما إذا اشترى لها شيئاً بعد ما بنى بها بأمرها أو دفع إليها دراهم حتى اشترت هي صرح به في التجنيس وفيه : لو قالت له أنفق على مماليكي من مهري ففعل ثم قالت لا أحسبه منه لأنك استخدمتهم، فما أنفق عليهم بالمعروف فهو من المهر . ولو بعث إليها بقرة عند موت أبيها فذبحتها وأطعمتها فطلب قيمتها فإن اتفقا أنه لم يذكر قيمة ليس له الرجوع، وإن اتفقا على ذكر الرجوع بالقيمة فله الرجوع، وإن اختلفا فالقول لها. واختار قاضيخان أن القول قول الزوج لأنها تدعي الإذن بالاستهلاك بغير عوض وهو ينكر فالقول له كمن دفع إلى غيره دراهم فأنفقها ثم ادعى أنها قرض وقال القابض إنها هبة
And a distinction is made between what preceded: in the first, the compensation from her was based on her assumption of his ownership, and he denied it, so the compensation was not valid, so it was not a gift from her, so she has the right to reclaim. In the second, ownership occurred, so the compensation was valid, so she has no recourse. And it may be said: the compensation is based on the assumption of the gift, not absolutely, and he has denied it, so she should have recourse. The author restricted it to his claiming it as mahr because if she claims it is from the mahr and he says it is a deposit, then if it is of the same type as the mahr, her statement is accepted; if it is of a different type, his statement is accepted. And he generalized in 'sending' to include when he bought something for her after consummation by her command, or gave her dirhams until she bought it herself—this is stated in al-Tajnīs. In it: if she said to him, 'Spend on my slaves from my mahr' and he did, then she said, 'I do not count it from it because you employed them,' then what he spent reasonably is from the mahr. And if he sent her a cow at the death of her father, and she slaughtered it and fed it, and he demanded its value: if they agree that he did not mention a value, he has no recourse; if they agree on mentioning recourse at value, he has recourse; if they differ, her statement is accepted. Qādī Khān chose that the husband's statement is accepted because she claims permission to consume without compensation and he denies, so his statement is accepted, like one who gives dirhams to another who spends them then claims they were a loan and the recipient says they were a gift.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage discusses scenarios where a wife sends compensation to her husband under the assumption that he had gifted her something, and later he claims that item was actually part of her mahr. The distinction lies in whether ownership occurred from the husband's side. If she sent compensation assuming he owned the item (i.e., she thought he gifted it), and he denies that ownership (claiming it was mahr), then the compensation fails and she can reclaim what she sent. If ownership actually occurred (e.g., he admitted gift), then the compensation is valid and she cannot reclaim. The author restricts this to the husband claiming it as mahr. If both claim differently (e.g., she says it was mahr, he says it was a deposit), then if the item matches the type of mahr, her claim is accepted; otherwise his. The passage also covers a cow sent by husband to wife at her father's death: if no value was mentioned, he cannot reclaim; if they agreed on recourse at value, he can; if dispute, wife's statement prevails. Qādī Khān prefers husband's statement by analogy with a loan vs. gift dispute.

Key Terms
تعويض (taʿwīḍ): Compensation given by the wife to the husband, often in exchange for an item she thought he gifted her.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A wife sends compensation to her husband for an item she assumed he owned. The husband later claims the item was part of the mahr, denying it was a gift. According to the passage, what is the ruling?
A) She cannot reclaim the compensation because it was a gift.
B) She can reclaim the compensation because the basis (gift) is denied.
C) The husband's claim is always accepted.
D) The item becomes joint property.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a wife's compensation hinges on the husband's acknowledgment of the item as a gift; if he denies, the compensation fails and she may reclaim.

Spinning Cotton Dispute and Invalid Hire Contracts
Ruling on a spin dispute and invalid hire contract

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that when a husband tells his wife 'spin it for us', the contract is invalid and she deserves ujrat al-mithl, and identify how the dispute about the verbal expression is resolved.

Core Idea
A hire for an uncertain share is invalid; the worker still gets fair wage for her labor.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq addresses a common household dispute: a husband gives his wife cotton to spin and later claims he said 'for us' (meaning they share the yarn) while she says 'for yourself'. The legal consequence depends on who is believed. The passage also clarifies the rule that a contract with an uncertain share (like the miller's qafiz) is invalid, but the woman is not left uncompensated – she receives ujrat al-mithl (fair wage) because she spun with the expectation of a share, not as a gift.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the claim
Determine the act: the wife spun cotton. The dispute is about the husband's words: did he say 'spin it for us' (meaning a share) or 'spin it for yourself'? The wife claims the former, the husband the latter.
The husband's word is taken with oath because the default is that he does not make a gift of his property.
2
Apply the rule for invalid hire
If he said 'spin it for us', the yarn is his but the contract is invalid because the wage (a share of the yarn) was uncertain, similar to the miller's measure (qafīz al-ṭaḥḥān).
Uncertainty in price invalidates hire (ijārah).
3
Determine the compensation
Despite the invalid contract, she deserves ujrat al-mithl because she worked expecting a share, not voluntarily.
Ujrat al-mithl is the fair market wage for similar work.
4
Note the dispute resolution
The husband's word is accepted with oath because the default presumption is that he does not give away his property. The wife must bring proof if she claims he said 'for us'.
This follows the principle of al‑qawl li‑man yunkir al‑tamām (the word of the one who denies an obligation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قال اغزليه (لنا أي لي ولك وقوله «فهو له» أي لأنه مستأجراً لها يصير بجزء منه فهو مثل قفيز الطحان فلم تصح الإجارة ويكون لها أجر مثلها لأنها غزلته على طمع أن لها منه حصة لا تبرعاً قوله كان للزوج أن يسترد ما دفع أي قائماً أو هالكاً لأنه رشوة كذا في البزازية قوله (وقيل لا يرجع الخ) حاصل القول الأول أنه يرجع مطلقاً سواء شرط التزوج أو لا، وسواء تزوجته أو لا، وحاصل الثاني أنه يرجع في صورة ما إذا أبت وكان شرط التزوج، أما إذا لم يشترط أو مطلقاً فلا رجوع له لأن قوله إذا زوجت الخ) يفهم منه عدم الرجوع إذا لم يشترط بالأولى، ويفهم من قوله وإن أبت الا أنه إن شرطه يرجع فصار حاصله ما قلنا وفي كلامه مخالفة لما في الفتح حيث قال : وفي الخلاصة أنفق على معتدة الغير على طمع أن يتزوجها إذا انقضت عدتها فلما انقضت أبت، إن شرط في الإنفاق التزوج يرجع زوجت نفسها أولاً لأنه رشوة والصحيح لا يرجع. لو زوجت نفسها وإن لم يشرط لكن أنفق على هذا الطمع اختلفوا والأصح أنه لا يرجع إذا زوجت. قاله الصدر الشهيد. وقال الشيخ الإمام: إنه يرجع عليها زوجت نفسها منه أو لا لأنه رشوة واختاره في المحيط، وهذا إذا دفع الدراهم إليها لتنفق على نفسها، أما إذا أكل معها فلا يرجع بشيء ا هـ . ولم يذكر ما إذا أبت أن تتزوجه في فصل عدم الاشتراط صريحاً إلا ما قد يتوهم من اقتصاره على قول الشهيد يرجع إذا لم تتزوجه وحكى في فتاوى الخاصي فيما إذا أنفق بلا شرط بل للعلم عرفاً أنه ينفق للتزوج ثم لم تتزوج به خلافاً، منهم من قال يرجع لأن المعروف كالمشروط، ومنهم من قال لا
And if he said 'spin it for us' meaning for me and for you, and his statement 'so it is for him' means because he made her employed so a part of it becomes his, so it is like the miller's measure, so the hire is invalid and she has the like of her wage because she spun it hoping to have a share from it, not voluntarily. His statement 'the husband can take back what he paid' i.e., whether it exists or is destroyed, because it is a bribe, as in al-Bazzāziyya. His statement 'and it is said he does not take back' etc. The gist of the first opinion is that he takes back absolutely, whether he stipulated marriage or not, and whether she married him or not. The gist of the second is that he takes back only when she refuses and he stipulated marriage. As for when he did not stipulate or absolutely, he has no recourse. Because his statement 'if she marries' etc. implies non-recourse if he did not stipulate a fortiori. And from his statement 'and if she refuses' it is understood that if he stipulated, he has recourse. So the conclusion is what we said. In his words there is contradiction with what is in al-Fatḥ where he said: In al-Khulāṣa: He spent on a woman in 'iddah of another hoping to marry her when her 'iddah ends. When it ended she refused. If he stipulated marriage in the spending, he has recourse whether she married herself or not because it is a bribe; the correct opinion is no recourse if she married herself. Even if he did not stipulate but spent on this hope, they differed; the most correct is that he has no recourse if she married herself. This was said by al-Ṣadr al-Shahīd. And Shaykh al-Imām said he has recourse against her whether she married him or not because it is a bribe, and he chose it in al-Muḥīṭ. This is if he gave her dirhams to spend on herself. But if he ate with her, he has no recourse at all. He did not mention explicitly when she refuses to marry him in the chapter of non-stipulation except what might be understood from his restriction to the Shahīd's saying that he has recourse if she does not marry him. And it was narrated in Fatāwā al-Khāṣī regarding one who spent without stipulation but it is customarily known that he spends for marriage, then she did not marry him: there is disagreement. Some said he has recourse because the customary is like stipulated; some said no.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage covers several subsidiary rulings. First, regarding spinning cotton: if a husband tells his wife 'spin it for us', the legal consequence is that the yarn belongs to him but she is entitled to a fair wage (ujrat al-mithl) because she spun expecting a share. If he says 'spin for yourself', it is hers. In disputes about what was said, the husband's word is taken with oath. Second, subsidiary issues: (1) a man pays money to a woman's brother under pressure to marry her; he can reclaim it as bribery. (2) A father demands advance payment of mahr, then gifts are sent but marriage fails; the man can reclaim gifts if they exist or if destroyed? The text says he can reclaim. (3) Spending on a woman in 'iddah of another with hope of marriage: if he stipulated marriage as a condition, he can reclaim the money because it is a bribe; but the correct view (as per al-Ṣadr al-Shahīd) is no recourse if she actually married him. If he did not stipulate, there is disagreement: some hold he can reclaim because custom acts like stipulation, others say no. The author prefers the latter (no recourse) except when stipulated and she refuses. The passage ends with a note that eating together forfeits recourse.

Key Terms
أجر مثلها (ujrat mithlihā): Fair market wage for similar work
قفيز الطحان (qafīz al-ṭaḥḥān): The miller's measure – a well-known example of an uncertain wage (a portion of the ground flour)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband tells his wife 'spin it for yourself'. She spins the cotton into yarn. According to the Hanafi school, who owns the yarn?
A) The husband, because the cotton was his.
B) The wife, because she spun it for herself.
C) They share equally.
D) The wife, but she must pay the value of the cotton.

Key Takeaway
A hire with an uncertain share is invalid, but the worker still receives a fair wage unless the work was done voluntarily.

Recovering Payments Made in Hope of Marriage
Rulings on reclaiming spending made in hope of marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that a man can reclaim money spent on a woman in 'iddah only if he explicitly stipulated marriage, and after performance, the preferred view denies recourse if she actually marries him.

Core Idea
Spending for marriage without condition is a gift; only an express stipulation creates a right to reclaim.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq tackles a delicate issue: a man spends money on a woman during her 'iddah hoping to marry her after her waiting period ends. When she refuses or marries someone else, can he take back what he gave? The Hanafi school is split, with al-Ṣadr al-Shahīd limiting recourse to cases where the man explicitly conditioned the spending on marriage and the woman refused, while Shaykh al-Imām allows recourse in all cases because the payment is considered a bribe. The author prefers the more restrictive view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the spending scenario
Determine whether the man gave money directly to the woman for her expenses, or ate with her (which forfeits recourse). The ruling applies only to direct monetary gifts.
Eating together is considered a mutual social act, not a bribe.
2
Check for an explicit stipulation
Did the man expressly condition the spending on marriage? If yes, proceed to step 3. If no, most Hanafis hold no recourse; a minority (Shaykh al-Imām) allow recourse because custom implies the condition.
The majority (al-Ṣadr al-Shahīd) require an explicit stipulation.
3
Apply the ruling based on the woman's response
If she refused to marry him, he can reclaim (because it is a bribe). If she married him (fulfilled the condition), the preferred view (al-Ṣadr al-Shahīd) says no recourse because the condition was satisfied.
Shaykh al-Imām would allow recourse even if she married him, but this is not mufta bihi.
4
Consider the effect of custom ('urf)
When no explicit stipulation exists but custom clearly shows the spending was for marriage, some Hanafis treat custom as a condition (al-maʻrūf ka al-mashrūṭ). However, the preferred view rejects this and treats it as a gift.
The author follows al-Ṣadr al-Shahīd in giving no weight to custom alone.
5
Identify the mufta bihi position
The operative ruling for fatwa: he can reclaim only if he stipulated marriage and she refused him; if she married him or if no stipulation, no recourse.
This is the view of al-Ṣadr al-Shahīd, adopted by the author.

A man spends on a woman in 'iddah without stipulating marriage. According to the mufta bihi view, can he reclaim the money if she later refuses him?
A) Yes, because custom shows his intention.
B) No, because it is considered a gift without condition.
C) Yes, because it is a bribe.
D) No, unless he stipulated.

Key Takeaway
Only an explicit condition to marry creates a right to reclaim spending; otherwise, the payment is a gift, and if she marries him, the condition is satisfied and no recourse.

Three Subsidiary Cases on Marriage Payments
Rulings on reclaiming payments made under duress, as advance mahr, or as bribe for marriage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on reclaiming payments made under duress, as advance mahr, or as bribes for marriage, and distinguish when restitution is possible.

Core Idea
Payments coerced, conditional on marriage, or designated as mahr are reclaimable; consumed gifts without condition are not.

The following three subsidiary cases, mentioned in the Hanafi fatāwā, clarify the legal status of various payments made before marriage. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for advising on marriage contracts and financial disputes. Each case illustrates a different scenario—duress, advance mahr, and conditional spending on a woman in 'iddah—and the rules for restitution.

Guided Path
1
Case 1: Payment under duress
Identify the scenario: a man is forced to pay dirhams to the woman's brother to secure marriage. After marriage, he reclaims the payment. The key factor is coercion—a bribe (rashwah). The ruling: the husband can reclaim the full amount, whether the money exists or not.
The recipient is the woman's brother, not the woman herself.
2
Case 2: Advance mahr payments
A father demands advance mahr before marrying his daughter. The suitor sends gifts designated for mahr and other gifts, but the marriage does not occur. Distinguish: items for mahr are reclaimable even if consumed; gifts sent as presents are reclaimable only if they still exist.
The key is the intention behind the payment (mahr vs. gift).
3
Case 3: Spending on a mu'taddah with expectation of marriage
A man spends on a woman in 'iddah hoping to marry her. Variations: with or without stipulation, and whether she marries him or refuses. The ruling depends on the condition and outcome. Understand the correct view (as-sahih) and the alternate view for each subcase.
Internal Hanafi disagreement exists on some subcases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وههنا فروع ذكروها في الفتاوى لا بأس بإيرادها فإنها مهمة : الأول لو خطب امرأة في بيت أخيها فأبى الأخ إلا أن يدفع إليه دراهم فدفع ثم تزوجها كان للزوج أن يسترد ما دفع له . الثاني لو خطب ابنة رجل فقال أبوها إن نقدت إلى المهر كذا أزوجها منك ثم بعد ذلك بعث بهدايا إلى بيت الأب ولم يقدر على أن ينقد المهر ولم يزوجه فأراد أن يرجع قالوا ما بعث للمهر وهو قائم أو هالك يسترده، وكذا كل ما بعث هدية وهو قائم فأما الهالك والمستهلك فلا شيء فيه. الثالث لو أنفق على معتدة الغير على طمع أن يتزوجها إذا انقضت عدتها فلما انقضت أبت ذلك إن شرط في الإنفاق التزوج كأن يقول أنفق بشرط أن تتزوجيني يرجع زوجت نفسها أو لا، وكذا إذا لم يشترط على الصحيح وقيل لا يرجع إذا زوجت نفسها وقد كان شرطه وصحح أيضاً، وإن أبت ولم يكن شرطه لا يرجع على الصحيح . تزوجته
And here are subsidiary issues they mentioned in the fatāwā; there is no harm in citing them as they are important: First: If a man proposed to a woman in her brother's house, and the brother refused unless he pays him dirhams, and he paid, then married her, the husband can reclaim what he paid. Second: If a man proposed to a man's daughter, and the father said, 'If you pay such-and-such as mahr, I will marry her to you,' then after that he sent gifts to the father's house and could not pay the mahr and he did not marry him, and he wanted to reclaim: they said what he sent for the mahr, whether existing or destroyed, he reclaims it; likewise everything sent as a gift that exists; as for what is destroyed or consumed, there is nothing. Third: If he spent on a woman in 'iddah of another hoping to marry her when her 'iddah ends, and when it ended she refused: if he stipulated marriage in the spending, e.g., he said 'I spend on condition that you marry me,' he can reclaim whether she married herself or not. Likewise if he did not stipulate, according to the correct view. And it was said he cannot reclaim if she married herself and he had stipulated, and that was also verified. And if she refused and he did not stipulate, he cannot reclaim according to the correct view.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage enumerates three important subsidiary rulings from Hanafi fatāwā. First, if a man is forced to pay money to a woman's brother to secure marriage, that payment is effectively a bribe (rishwah). After marriage, he can reclaim it from the brother, regardless of whether the money still exists or was spent. Second, when a father demands advance payment of mahr before marrying his daughter, and the suitor sends gifts for the mahr but then cannot complete the marriage, he can reclaim items sent specifically for mahr (whether existing or consumed) and also gifts that are still in existence. Consumed gifts cannot be reclaimed. Third, a man who spends on a woman in 'iddah hoping to marry her: if he explicitly stipulated marriage as a condition, he can reclaim the money whether she marries him or not. Even if he did not stipulate, the correct view (sahīh) is that he can reclaim if she refuses. However, if she marries him after he stipulated, some say he cannot reclaim because the condition is fulfilled; that view is also verified. But if he did not stipulate and she refuses, he cannot reclaim on the correct view.

Key Terms
رشوة (rashwah): Bribe, payment to facilitate marriage; Hanafi: recoverable
معتدة (muʿtaddah): A woman observing 'iddah (waiting period) after divorce or death

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man proposes to a woman and her father demands advance payment of mahr. The man sends gifts but cannot complete the marriage. Can he reclaim the gifts?
A) Yes, only if they still exist.
B) Yes, whether they exist or are consumed, if they were for mahr.
C) No, gifts are never reclaimable.
D) Only if the father agrees.

Key Takeaway
Coercive or conditional marriage payments are reclaimable, but voluntary gifts are only reclaimable while they still exist.
