Ruling on Wife Spinning Husband's Cotton: Ownership and Compensation
Ruling on wife spinning husband's cotton.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ownership of spun thread and the compensation rules based on the husband's instructions.

Core Idea
The husband's words determine ownership of the thread and the wife's wage or liability.

This lesson examines the detailed ruling from Qadi Khan on a common household scenario: a wife spins cotton brought by her husband. The Hanafi fiqh provides nuanced rules depending on the husband's explicit words, his silence, or his prohibition. Understanding these distinctions is essential for resolving disputes over property and labor in marriage.

Guided Path
1
Identify the husband's statement
The first condition is the exact wording used by the husband. The text lists several possibilities: 'spin for me', 'spin for us', 'spin', 'spin for yourself', or silence.
2
Determine ownership of the thread
Based on the husband's statement, who owns the spun thread? For example, 'spin for me' gives ownership to husband; 'spin for yourself' gives to wife.
3
Determine the wife's wage or liability
If the husband forbids spinning, the wife becomes a usurper and must compensate for the cotton but keeps the thread. If not forbidden, wage rules apply based on statement.
4
Handle cases of no instruction
If the husband neither forbids nor permits, and he is a cotton seller, thread belongs to wife with compensation; otherwise to husband.
5
Resolve disputes
In disputes over the words, the husband's statement is taken with his oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي فتاوى قاضيخان لو جاء إلى بيته بقطن فغزلته المرأة فإن قال : اغزليه لي فهو له ولا أجر لها، وإن قال اغزليه لنا فهو له ولها أجر مثلها، وإن قال اغزليه فهو له وإن قال اغزليه لنفسك فهو لها، وإن اختلفا فقالت قلت اغزليه لنفسك وكذبها فالقول قوله مع يمينه وإن نهاها عن غزله فغزلته كان لها لأنها غاصبة وله عليها مثل قطنه، وإن اختلفا في النهي فالقول له وإن لم ينه ولم يأذن فغزلته إن كان بياع القطن فهو لها وعليها مثل قطنه وإلا فهو له إلى أخر ما في الفتاوى وههنا فروع ذكروها في الفتاوى لا بأس بإيرادها فإنها مهمة : الأول لو خطب امرأة في بيت أخيها فأبى الأخ إلا أن يدفع إليه دراهم فدفع ثم تزوجها كان للزوج أن يسترد ما دفع له . الثاني لو خطب ابنة رجل فقال أبوها إن نقدت إلى المهر كذا أزوجها منك ثم بعد ذلك بعث بهدايا إلى بيت الأب ولم يقدر على أن ينقد المهر ولم يزوجه فأراد أن يرجع قالوا ما بعث للمهر وهو قائم أو هالك يسترده، وكذا كل ما بعث هدية وهو قائم فأما الهالك والمستهلك فلا شيء فيه. الثالث لو أنفق على معتدة الغير على طمع أن يتزوجها إذا انقضت عدتها فلما انقضت أبت ذلك إن شرط في الإنفاق التزوج كأن يقول أنفق بشرط أن تتزوجيني يرجع زوجت نفسها أو لا، وكذا إذا لم يشترط على الصحيح وقيل لا يرجع إذا زوجت نفسها وقد كان شرطه وصحح أيضاً، وإن أبت ولم يكن شرطه لا يرجع على الصحيح . الرابع مسألة الجهاز وفيه مسألتان : الأولى قال في المبتغى بالغين المعجمة : من زفت إليه امرأته بلا جهاز فله مطالبة الأب بما بعث إليه من الدنانير والدراهم، وإن كان الجهاز قليلاً فله المطالبة بما يليق بالمبعوث يعني إذا لم تجهر بما يليق بالمبعوث فله استرداد ما بعث، والمعتبر ما يتخذ للزوج لا ما يتخذ لها، ولو سكت بعد الزفاف طويلاً ليس له أن يخاصمه بعده وإن لم يتخذ له شيء. ولو جهز ابنته وسلمه إليها ليس له في الاستحسان استرداده منها وعليه الفتوى، ولو أخذ أهل المرأة شيئاً عند التسليم فللزوج أن يسترده لأنه رشوة. الثانية لو جهز بنته ثم ادعى أن ما دفعه لها عارية وقالت تمليكاً أو قال الزوج ذلك بعد موتها ليرث منه وقال الأب عارية، ففي فتح القدير والتجنيس والذخيرة المختار للفتوى أن القول للزوج ولها إذا كان العرف مستمراً أن الأب يدفع مثله جهازاً لا عارية كما في ديارنا ، وإن كان مشتركاً فالقول قول الأب. وقال قاضيخان : وينبغي أن يكون الجواب على التفصيل إن كان الأب من الأشراف والكرام لا يقبل قوله أنه عارية، وإن كان الأب ممن لا يجهز البنات بمثل ذلك قبل قوله اهـ. والواقع في ديارنا القاهرة أن العرف مشترك فيفتى بأن القول للأب وإذا كان القول للزوج في الأولى فأقام الأب بينة قبلت قال في التجنيس والولوالجية والذخيرة: والبينة الصحيحة أن يشهد عند التسليم إلى المرأة أني إنما سلمت هذه الأشياء بطريق العارية أو يكتب نسخة معلومة ويشهد الأب على إقرارها أن جميع ما في هذه النسخة ملك والدي عارية في يدي منه لكن هذا يصلح للقضاء لا للاحتياط لجواز أنه اشترى لها بعض هذه الأشياء في حالة الصغر فبهذا الإقرار لا يصير الأب صادقاً فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى والاحتياط أن يشتري منها ما في هذه النسخة بثمن معلوم ثم إن البنت تبرئه عن الثمن .اهـ. ومن فروع الجهاز لو زوج ابنته البالغة وجهزها بأمتعة معينة ولم يسلمها إليها ثم فسخ العقد وزوجها من آخر فليس لها مطالبة الأب بذلك الجهاز لأن التجهيز تمليك فيشترط فيه التسليم، ولو كان لها على أبيها دين فجهزها أبوها ثم قال جهزتها بدينها علي وقالت بل بمالك فالقول للأب، وقيل للبنت. ولو دفع إلى أم ولده شيئاً لتتخذه جهازاً للبنت ففعلت وسلمته إليها لا . يصح تسليمها. صغيرة المسألة قال : وهو الصحيح لأنه إنما أنفق على قصده لا بشرطه .اهـ كلام الفتح. قلت: والمفهوم منه أن الصحيح أنه لا يرجع فيما إذا تزوجته مطلقاً شرط الرجوع أو لا، ويرجع فيما إذا أبت مطلقاً وهذا هو المفهوم من العمادية أيضاً، وما ذكره المؤلف من القول الثاني مخالف لهما فلينظر من أين أخذه. وأما ما ذكره من القول الأول فهو موافق لإطلاق ما تقدم عن الشيخ الإمام الذي اختاره في المحيط قوله (ليس له في الاستحسان أي ليس للأب قوله وقال قاضيخان وينبغي أن يكون الخ) قال في
In the fatwas of Qadi Khan: If a man brings cotton to his home and his wife spins it. If he says: 'Spin it for me', then it belongs to him and she has no wage. If he says: 'Spin it for us', then it belongs to him, and she has a wage like her. If he says: 'Spin it', then it belongs to him. And if he says: 'Spin it for yourself', then it belongs to her. If they dispute and she says: 'I said spin it for yourself', and he denies her, then the statement is his with his oath. If he forbids her from spinning it and she spins it, then it belongs to her because she is a usurper, and he has a like of his cotton from her. If they dispute over the prohibition, then the statement is his. If he did not forbid nor permit, and she spun it, then if he is a cotton seller, it belongs to her and she must give a like of his cotton; otherwise, it belongs to him. Up to the end of what is in the fatwas. Here are sub-issues mentioned in the fatwas; it is good to mention them as they are important. First: If a man seeks marriage with a woman in her brother's house, and the brother refuses to give her except in exchange for money given to him, so he gives it, then marries her, the husband may take back what he gave. Second: If a man seeks marriage with a man's daughter, and her father says: 'If you pay such-and-such as dower, I will marry her to you', then after that he sends gifts to the father's house, but he cannot pay the dower and does not marry her, and he wants to take back the gifts, they said: what he sent for the dower, whether in existence or destroyed, he takes it back. Similarly, all that he sent as a gift while it is in existence; but as for destroyed or consumed, nothing is due. Third: If a man spends on another's woman in iddah hoping to marry her when her iddah ends, and when it ends she refuses. If he made a condition in the spending that she marry him, such as saying: 'I spend on condition that you marry me', he takes back the spending whether she marries him or not. Similarly, if he did not make a condition—according to the sound opinion he takes back; and it was said he does not take back if she marries herself, even if he had conditioned, and this was also declared sound. And if she refuses and he had not conditioned, he does not take back according to the sound opinion. Fourth: The issue of trousseau (jihaz). It contains two questions. First: Al-Mubtagha says: One whose wife is brought to him without a trousseau may demand from the father what he sent of dinars and dirhams. If the trousseau is insufficient, he may demand what is befitting for what he sent—meaning if she does not provide a trousseau befitting what he sent, he may take back what he sent. What is considered is what is prepared for the husband, not what is prepared for her. If he remains silent for a long time after the wedding, he may not litigate thereafter, even if nothing was prepared for him. If a father provides a trousseau for his daughter and hands it over to her, he may not, by istihsan, take it back from her, and fatwa is given accordingly. If the woman's family takes something upon delivery, the husband may take it back because it is bribery. Second: If a father provides a trousseau for his daughter, then claims that what he gave her was a loan ('ariyah), while she says it was a gift (tamlik); or the husband says so after her death to inherit from it, and the father says it was a loan, then in Fath al-Qadir, al-Tajnis, and al-Dhakhira, the chosen position for fatwa is that the statement is of the husband and her, if the custom is continuous that the father gives the like as a trousseau not as a loan, as in our lands. If custom is mixed, then the statement is of the father. Qadi Khan said: The answer should be detailed: if the father is from the nobles and generous, his claim that it is a loan is not accepted; if the father is from those who do not provide trousseau for daughters, his statement is accepted. The reality in our lands of Cairo is that custom is mixed, so fatwa is given that the statement is of the father. And if the statement is of the husband in the first case, and the father brings evidence, it is accepted. In al-Tajnis, al-Walwaljiah, and al-Dhakhira: The valid evidence is that he bear witness at the time of delivery to the woman that he handed over these items as a loan, or he writes a known copy and has the father witness her acknowledgement that all in this copy is the property of my father as a loan in my possession. But this suffices for the court, not for piety, because it is possible that he bought some of these items for her while she was a minor; with this acknowledgement the father does not become truthful in what is between him and Allah. The pious precaution is that he buys from her what is in this copy for a known price, then the daughter releases him from the price. Among the sub-issues of trousseau: If a father marries off his adult daughter and provides a trousseau of specific items but does not hand them over to her, then the marriage is annulled and he marries her to another, she cannot demand that trousseau from her father, because providing a trousseau is a gift, so delivery is required. If she had a debt on her father and he provides a trousseau, then says: I provided it as payment for her debt, and she says: Rather from your own wealth, the statement is of the father; and it was said for the daughter. If he gives something to his umm walad to prepare it as a trousseau for the daughter, and she does so and hands it over to her, her delivery is not valid. The minor issue. He said: It is the sound position, because he only spent for his intention, not on condition. End of the words of al-Fath. I say: What is understood from it is that the sound opinion is that he does not take back in any case when she marries him, whether he conditioned return or not, and he takes back when she refuses in any case. This is also what is understood from al-Imadiyya. What the author mentioned as the second opinion contradicts these, so let it be examined whence he took it. As for the first opinion he mentioned, it is in agreement with the absolute statement of al-Shaykh al-Imam, which he chose in al-Muhit. His statement: 'He does not have by istihsan' meaning that the father does not have. His statement: 'Qadi Khan said: The answer should be detailed...' He said in
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq gathers several important rulings from fatwa collections on disputes arising from marriage-related financial transactions. The passage type is an expansion of rulings (expansion_probe). The author first presents the famous ruling from Qadi Khan on a wife spinning cotton brought by her husband. The key principle is that the husband's statement is taken over the wife's in disputes about the instruction, with his oath. The wife becomes a usurper if she spins against a prohibition, and the thread belongs to her but she must compensate for the cotton. Different scenarios are delineated based on the husband's explicit words. Then, several sub-issues (furu') are listed. The first concerns paying a brother to secure marriage: such payment is considered bribery (rishwa), and the husband may recover it. The second deals with gifts sent to the father as part of the dower: if the marriage does not occur, the gifts are recoverable if they still exist, but not if consumed. The third is a detailed case of spending on a woman in 'iddah hoping to marry her: the ruling hinges on whether a condition was made. According to the sound opinion, if the condition is explicit, the spender can recover his spending whether the woman marries him or not; if no condition was made, the rulings differ, but the relied-upon position (as per al-Fath and al-Imadiyya) is that he cannot recover if she marries him, but can recover if she refuses. The fourth issue concerns the bridal trousseau (jihaz). The first sub-issue gives the husband the right to demand a proper trousseau befitting what he sent, and if not provided, he may take back what he sent. However, if he remains silent for a long time after the wedding, he loses this right. The second sub-issue addresses the common dispute: is the father's delivery of the trousseau a gift or a loan? The ruling depends on custom (urf). In lands where the custom is for the father to give a gift, the husband's or wife's claim that it is a gift is accepted; if custom is mixed, the father's claim of a loan is believed. The author also discusses the role of evidence and the preferable form of proof to avoid sin. Finally, additional sub-rulings on trousseau are provided, such as when a father provides a trousseau before delivery and the marriage is annulled, or when the father claims the trousseau was in lieu of a debt. All these rules reflect the Hanafi school's reliance on custom, conditions, and the principle that in financial disputes the party claiming the default (bara'ah) or the one whose statement is supported by apparent circumstances is believed.

Key Terms
جهاز (jihāz): Trousseau: items (furniture, clothes, etc.) that the bride's family provides for the new household.
معتدة الغير (muʿtaddat al-ghayr): A woman observing the waiting period (ʿiddah) after divorce or death of another man (not her husband).
رشوة (rishwah): Bribe: any payment made to secure a marriage or any benefit that is unjust.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the husband says 'Spin it for us' and the wife spins, what is the ruling?
A) The husband owns the thread and must pay the wife a wage
B) The wife owns the thread and the husband must pay her
C) They share the thread equally
D) The husband owns the thread and no wage is owed

Key Takeaway
The husband's explicit instruction determines ownership of spun thread and the wife's entitlement to wages or compensation.

Nikah — Marriage
intermediate
6 min
Bribery and Gifts for Marriage: Recovery Rights
Rulings on bribery and gifts in marriage negotiations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings on recovering payments made to secure a marriage and gifts sent as part of the dower.

Core Idea
Payments made to secure marriage against the woman's family's consent are recoverable, and gifts for dower are recoverable if the marriage fails, subject to existence.

This lesson covers two sub-issues from the fatwa collections: paying a brother to allow marriage, and sending gifts to the father as part of the dower agreement. The Hanafi school distinguishes between bribery (which is prohibited but recoverable) and gifts that are conditional upon the marriage taking place. Understanding these rules protects individuals from financial loss in failed marriage negotiations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first scenario: paying the brother
A man pays the brother of a woman to secure her marriage. The payment is considered bribery (rishwa).
2
Determine if the payment is recoverable
If the marriage takes place, the husband can take back what he paid because it was a bribe, not valid consideration.
3
Identify the second scenario: gifts to the father
A man sends gifts to the father on the promise of marriage for a specified dower, but then cannot pay the dower and the marriage does not occur.
4
Distinguish between dower gifts and non-dower gifts
Gifts sent for the dower are recoverable whether existing or destroyed. General gifts are recoverable only if they still exist.

وفي فتاوى قاضيخان لو جاء إلى بيته بقطن فغزلته المرأة فإن قال : اغزليه لي فهو له ولا أجر لها، وإن قال اغزليه لنا فهو له ولها أجر مثلها، وإن قال اغزليه فهو له وإن قال اغزليه لنفسك فهو لها، وإن اختلفا In the fatwas of Qadi Khan: If a man brings cotton to his home and his wife spins it. If he says: 'Spin it for me', then it belongs to him and she has no wage. If he says: 'Spin it for us', then it belongs to him, and she has a wage like her. If he says: 'Spin it', then it belongs to him. And if he says: 'Spin it for yourself', then it belongs to her. If they dispute and she says: 'I said spin it for yourself', and he denies her, then the statement is his with his oath. If he forbids her from spinning it and she spins it, then it belongs to her because she is a usurper, and he has a like of his cotton from her. If they dispute over the prohibition, then the statement is his. If he did not forbid nor permit, and she spun it, then if he is a cotton seller, it belongs to her and she must give a like of his cotton; otherwise, it belongs to him. Up to the end of what is in the fatwas. Here are sub-issues mentioned in the fatwas; it is good to mention them as they are important. First: If a man seeks marriage with a woman in her brother's house, and the brother refuses to give her except in exchange for money given to him, so he gives it, then marries her, the husband may take back what he gave. Second: If a man seeks marriage with a man's daughter, and her father says: 'If you pay such-and-such as dower, I will marry her to you', then after that he sends gifts to the father's house, but he cannot pay the dower and does not marry her, and he wants to take back the gifts, they said: what he sent for the dower, whether in existence or destroyed, he takes it back. Similarly, all that he sent as a gift while it is in existence; but as for destroyed or consumed, nothing is due. Third: If a man spends on another's woman in iddah hoping to marry her when her iddah ends, and when it ends she refuses. If he made a condition in the spending that she marry him, such as saying: 'I spend on condition that you marry me', he takes back the spending whether she marries him or not. Similarly, if he did not make a condition—according to the sound opinion he takes back; and it was said he does not take back if she marries herself, even if he had conditioned, and this was also declared sound. And if she refuses and he had not conditioned, he does not take back according to the sound opinion. Fourth: The issue of trousseau (jihaz). It contains two questions. First: Al-Mubtagha says: One whose wife is brought to him without a trousseau may demand from the father what he sent of dinars and dirhams. If the trousseau is insufficient, he may demand what is befitting for what he sent—meaning if she does not provide a trousseau befitting what he sent, he may take back what he sent. What is considered is what is prepared for the husband, not what is prepared for her. If he remains silent for a long time after the wedding, he may not litigate thereafter, even if nothing was prepared for him. If a father provides a trousseau for his daughter and hands it over to her, he may not, by istihsan, take it back from her, and fatwa is given accordingly. If the woman's family takes something upon delivery, the husband may take it back because it is bribery. Second: If a father provides a trousseau for his daughter, then claims that what he gave her was a loan ('ariyah), while she says it was a gift (tamlik); or the husband says so after her death to inherit from it, and the father says it was a loan, then in Fath al-Qadir, al-Tajnis, and al-Dhakhira, the chosen position for fatwa is that the statement is of the husband and her, if the custom is continuous that the father gives the like as a trousseau not as a loan, as in our lands. If custom is mixed, then the statement is of the father. Qadi Khan said: The answer should be detailed: if the father is from the nobles and generous, his claim that it is a loan is not accepted; if the father is from those who do not provide trousseau for daughters, his statement is accepted. The reality in our lands of Cairo is that custom is mixed, so fatwa is given that the statement is of the father. And if the statement is of the husband in the first case, and the father brings evidence, it is accepted. In al-Tajnis, al-Walwaljiah, and al-Dhakhira: The valid evidence is that he bear witness at the time of delivery to the woman that he handed over these items as a loan, or he writes a known copy and has the father witness her acknowledgement that all in this copy is the property of my father as a loan in my possession. But this suffices for the court, not for piety, because it is possible that he bought some of these items for her while she was a minor; with this acknowledgement the father does not become truthful in what is between him and Allah. The pious precaution is that he buys from her what is in this copy for a known price, then the daughter releases him from the price. Among the sub-issues of trousseau: If a father marries off his adult daughter and provides a trousseau of specific items but does not hand them over to her, then the marriage is annulled and he marries her to another, she cannot demand that trousseau from her father, because providing a trousseau is a gift, so delivery is required. If she had a debt on her father and he provides a trousseau, then says: I provided it as payment for her debt, and she says: Rather from your own wealth, the statement is of the father; and it was said for the daughter. If he gives something to his umm walad to prepare it as a trousseau for the daughter, and she does so and hands it over to her, her delivery is not valid. The minor issue. He said: It is the sound position, because he only spent for his intention, not on condition. End of the words of al-Fath. I say: What is understood from it is that the sound opinion is that he does not take back in any case when she marries him, whether he conditioned return or not, and he takes back when she refuses in any case. This is also what is understood from al-Imadiyya. What the author mentioned as the second opinion contradicts these, so let it be examined whence he took it. As for the first opinion he mentioned, it is in agreement with the absolute statement of al-Shaykh al-Imam, which he chose in al-Muhit. His statement: 'He does not have by istihsan' meaning that the father does not have. His statement: 'Qadi Khan said: The answer should be detailed...' He said in
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq gathers several important rulings from fatwa collections on disputes arising from marriage-related financial transactions. The passage type is an expansion of rulings (expansion_probe). The author first presents the famous ruling from Qadi Khan on a wife spinning cotton brought by her husband. The key principle is that the husband's statement is taken over the wife's in disputes about the instruction, with his oath. The wife becomes a usurper if she spins against a prohibition, and the thread belongs to her but she must compensate for the cotton. Different scenarios are delineated based on the husband's explicit words. Then, several sub-issues (furu') are listed. The first concerns paying a brother to secure marriage: such payment is considered bribery (rishwa), and the husband may recover it. The second deals with gifts sent to the father as part of the dower: if the marriage does not occur, the gifts are recoverable if they still exist, but not if consumed. The third is a detailed case of spending on a woman in 'iddah hoping to marry her: the ruling hinges on whether a condition was made. According to the sound opinion, if the condition is explicit, the spender can recover his spending whether the woman marries him or not; if no condition was made, the rulings differ, but the relied-upon position (as per al-Fath and al-Imadiyya) is that he cannot recover if she marries him, but can recover if she refuses. The fourth issue concerns the bridal trousseau (jihaz). The first sub-issue gives the husband the right to demand a proper trousseau befitting what he sent, and if not provided, he may take back what he sent. However, if he remains silent for a long time after the wedding, he loses this right. The second sub-issue addresses the common dispute: is the father's delivery of the trousseau a gift or a loan? The ruling depends on custom (urf). In lands where the custom is for the father to give a gift, the husband's or wife's claim that it is a gift is accepted; if custom is mixed, the father's claim of a loan is believed. The author also discusses the role of evidence and the preferable form of proof to avoid sin. Finally, additional sub-rulings on trousseau are provided, such as when a father provides a trousseau before delivery and the marriage is annulled, or when the father claims the trousseau was in lieu of a debt. All these rules reflect the Hanafi school's reliance on custom, conditions, and the principle that in financial disputes the party claiming the default (bara'ah) or the one whose statement is supported by apparent circumstances is believed.

Key Terms
جهاز (jihāz): Trousseau: items (furniture, clothes, etc.) that the bride's family provides for the new household.
معتدة الغير (muʿtaddat al-ghayr): A woman observing the waiting period (ʿiddah) after divorce or death of another man (not her husband).
رشوة (rishwah): Bribe: any payment made to secure a marriage or any benefit that is unjust.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man sends a gift to the bride's father specifically as part of the dower, and the marriage does not occur, is the gift recoverable if it has been destroyed?
A) Yes, because it was for the dower
B) No, because it is destroyed
C) Only if he had a written contract
D) Only if the father agrees

Key Takeaway
Payments made under coercion or condition are recoverable; the nature of the gift determines the terms of recovery.

Spending on a Woman in 'Iddah: Condition and Recovery
Ruling on recovering spending on a woman in iddah with or without condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the rules of recovering spending on a woman in iddah based on whether a condition to marry was made, and whether she marries him or refuses.

Core Idea
Recovery of spending on a woman in iddah depends on explicit conditions and her response; if she refuses despite a condition, he recovers; if she marries him unconditionally, he does not recover.

This lesson addresses the third sub-issue: a man spends on a woman who is in the iddah of another man, hoping to marry her when her iddah ends. The ruling varies depending on whether he made a condition that she marry him and whether she actually marries him or refuses. The Hanafi school has multiple, but the relied-upon position from al-Fath and al-Imadiyya is presented.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic scenario
A man spends on a woman in iddah (not his wife) hoping to marry her. After iddah, she refuses or marries him.
2
Check if a condition was made
Did he explicitly condition the spending on her marrying him? If yes, he can recover regardless of her consent. If no, the ruling depends on her action.
3
Determine the outcome if condition was made
If condition was made, he recovers his spending whether she marries him or not. If she marries him, he still recovers (because the condition was fulfilled? Actually, the text says he recovers absolutely).
Some opinions: if she marries him, he does not recover.
4
Determine the outcome if no condition was made
If he did not condition and she refuses, he does not recover. If he did not condition and she marries him, he does not recover (according to the sound opinion).
There is an opinion that if she marries him unconditionally, he recovers, but the relied-upon view is he does not.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The author notes that the author of the main text (al-Quduri?) mentioned a second opinion that contradicts the relied-upon view, and he criticizes it.

الشيخ الإمام الذي اختاره في المحيط قوله (ليس له في الاستحسان أي ليس للأب قوله وقال قاضيخان وينبغي أن يكون الخ) قال في
In the fatwas of Qadi Khan: If a man brings cotton to his home and his wife spins it. If he says: 'Spin it for me', then it belongs to him and she has no wage. If he says: 'Spin it for us', then it belongs to him, and she has a wage like her. If he says: 'Spin it', then it belongs to him. And if he says: 'Spin it for yourself', then it belongs to her. If they dispute and she says: 'I said spin it for yourself', and he denies her, then the statement is his with his oath. If he forbids her from spinning it and she spins it, then it belongs to her because she is a usurper, and he has a like of his cotton from her. If they dispute over the prohibition, then the statement is his. If he did not forbid nor permit, and she spun it, then if he is a cotton seller, it belongs to her and she must give a like of his cotton; otherwise, it belongs to him. Up to the end of what is in the fatwas. Here are sub-issues mentioned in the fatwas; it is good to mention them as they are important. First: If a man seeks marriage with a woman in her brother's house, and the brother refuses to give her except in exchange for money given to him, so he gives it, then marries her, the husband may take back what he gave. Second: If a man seeks marriage with a man's daughter, and her father says: 'If you pay such-and-such as dower, I will marry her to you', then after that he sends gifts to the father's house, but he cannot pay the dower and does not marry her, and he wants to take back the gifts, they said: what he sent for the dower, whether in existence or destroyed, he takes it back. Similarly, all that he sent as a gift while it is in existence; but as for destroyed or consumed, nothing is due. Third: If a man spends on another's woman in iddah hoping to marry her when her iddah ends, and when it ends she refuses. If he made a condition in the spending that she marry him, such as saying: 'I spend on condition that you marry me', he takes back the spending whether she marries him or not. Similarly, if he did not make a condition—according to the sound opinion he takes back; and it was said he does not take back if she marries herself, even if he had conditioned, and this was also declared sound. And if she refuses and he had not conditioned, he does not take back according to the sound opinion. Fourth: The issue of trousseau (jihaz). It contains two questions. First: Al-Mubtagha says: One whose wife is brought to him without a trousseau may demand from the father what he sent of dinars and dirhams. If the trousseau is insufficient, he may demand what is befitting for what he sent—meaning if she does not provide a trousseau befitting what he sent, he may take back what he sent. What is considered is what is prepared for the husband, not what is prepared for her. If he remains silent for a long time after the wedding, he may not litigate thereafter, even if nothing was prepared for him. If a father provides a trousseau for his daughter and hands it over to her, he may not, by istihsan, take it back from her, and fatwa is given accordingly. If the woman's family takes something upon delivery, the husband may take it back because it is bribery. Second: If a father provides a trousseau for his daughter, then claims that what he gave her was a loan ('ariyah), while she says it was a gift (tamlik); or the husband says so after her death to inherit from it, and the father says it was a loan, then in Fath al-Qadir, al-Tajnis, and al-Dhakhira, the chosen position for fatwa is that the statement is of the husband and her, if the custom is continuous that the father gives the like as a trousseau not as a loan, as in our lands. If custom is mixed, then the statement is of the father. Qadi Khan said: The answer should be detailed: if the father is from the nobles and generous, his claim that it is a loan is not accepted; if the father is from those who do not provide trousseau for daughters, his statement is accepted. The reality in our lands of Cairo is that custom is mixed, so fatwa is given that the statement is of the father. And if the statement is of the husband in the first case, and the father brings evidence, it is accepted. In al-Tajnis, al-Walwaljiah, and al-Dhakhira: The valid evidence is that he bear witness at the time of delivery to the woman that he handed over these items as a loan, or he writes a known copy and has the father witness her acknowledgement that all in this copy is the property of my father as a loan in my possession. But this suffices for the court, not for piety, because it is possible that he bought some of these items for her while she was a minor; with this acknowledgement the father does not become truthful in what is between him and Allah. The pious precaution is that he buys from her what is in this copy for a known price, then the daughter releases him from the price. If she had a debt on her father and he provides a trousseau, then says: I provided it as payment for her debt, and she says: Rather from your own wealth, the statement is of the father; and it was said for the daughter. If he gives something to his umm walad to prepare it as a trousseau for the daughter, and she does so and hands it over to her, her delivery is not valid. The minor issue. He said: It is the sound position, because he only spent for his intention, not on condition. End of the words of al-Fath. I say: What is understood from it is that the sound opinion is that he does not take back in any case when she marries him, whether he conditioned return or not, and he takes back when she refuses in any case. As for the first opinion he mentioned, it is in agreement with the absolute statement of al-Shaykh al-Imam, which he chose in al-Muhit. Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view, if a man spends on a woman in iddah without condition and she marries him, can he recover the spending?
A) Yes, because the marriage occurred
B) No, because there was no condition
C) Yes, but only if they divorce later
D) No, unless he had witnesses

Nikah — Marriage
advanced

Trousseau (Jihaz) Disputes: Gift or Loan?
Rulings on trousseau disputes: demand for proper trousseau and gift vs loan dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rules on trousseau disputes, including the role of custom (urf) and the father's right to reclaim, and apply the rules to scenarios involving evidence and silence.

Core Idea
Custom determines whether the father's trousseau is a gift or loan; the husband can demand a proper trousseau but must act promptly.

This lesson addresses the fourth and most detailed sub-issue: the bridal trousseau (jihaz). The passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq presents two main questions: the husband's right to demand a trousseau befitting his gifts, and the common dispute between father and daughter (or husband) over whether the trousseau was a gift or a loan. The rulings heavily rely on custom (urf) and the principle that the party whose claim aligns with the apparent custom is believed.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first question: husband's right to trousseau
If the wife is brought without a trousseau, the husband may demand from the father what he sent (money or goods). If the trousseau is insufficient, he may demand a proper one.
What is considered is what is prepared for the husband, not for the wife.
2
Note the effect of silence
If the husband remains silent for a long time after the wedding, he loses his right to litigate.
3
Identify the second question: gift vs loan dispute
After the father provides the trousseau, a dispute arises: the father claims it was a loan (ariyah), while the wife (or husband after her death) claims it was a gift (tamlik).
4
Apply the rule based on custom
If the continuous custom (urf) is that fathers give such items as gifts, the statement of the wife/ husband is believed. If custom is mixed, the father's statement is taken.
Qadi Khan adds a detail: if the father is from the generous nobility, his claim of loan is not accepted.
5
Examine the role of evidence
If the father brings evidence (bainah) that it was a loan, it is accepted. The best evidence is a written acknowledgment at the time of delivery. However, such evidence works in court but may not be valid before Allah if the father actually intended a gift.
A pious precaution is for the father to buy the items from the daughter and release the debt.

Source Text

وفي فتاوى قاضيخان لو جاء إلى بيته بقطن فغزلته المرأة فإن قال : اغزليه لي فهو له ولا أجر لها، وإن قال اغزليه لنا فهو له ولها أجر مثلها، وإن قال اغزليه فهو له وإن قال اغزليه لنفسك فهو لها، وإن اختلفا بما يليق بالمبعوث فله استرداد ما بعث، والمعتبر ما يتخذ للزوج لا ما يتخذ لها، ولو سكت بعد الزفاف طويلاً ليس له أن يخاصمه بعده وإن لم يتخذ له شيء. ولو جهز ابنته وسلمه إليها ليس له في الاستحسان استرداده منها وعليه الفتوى، ولو أخذ أهل المرأة شيئاً عند التسليم فللزوج أن يسترده لأنه رشوة. الثانية لو جهز بنته ثم ادعى أن ما دفعه لها عارية وقالت تمليكاً أو قال الزوج ذلك بعد موتها ليرث منه وقال الأب عارية، ففي فتح القدير والتجنيس والذخيرة المختار للفتوى أن القول للزوج ولها إذا كان العرف مستمراً أن الأب يدفع مثله جهازاً لا عارية كما في ديارنا ، وإن كان مشتركاً فالقول قول الأب. وقال قاضيخان : وينبغي أن كان القول للزوج في الأولى فأقام الأب بينة قبلت قال في التجنيس والولوالجية والذخيرة: والبينة الصحيحة أن يشهد عند التسليم إلى المرأة أني إنما سلمت هذه الأشياء بطريق العارية أو يكتب نسخة معلومة ويشهد الأب على إقرارها أن جميع ما في هذه النسخة ملك والدي عارية في يدي منه لكن هذا يصلح للقضاء لا للاحتياط لجواز أنه اشترى لها بعض هذه الأشياء في حالة الصغر فبهذا الإقرار لا يصير الأب صادقاً فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى والاحتياط أن يشتري منها ما في هذه النسخة بثمن معلوم ثم إن البنت تبرئه عن الثمن .اهـ. ومن فروع الجهاز لو زوج ابنته البالغة وجهزها بأمتعة معينة ولم يسلمها إليها ثم فسخ العقد وزوجها من آخر فليس لها مطالبة الأب بذلك الجهاز لأن التجهيز تمليك فيشترط فيه التسليم، ولو كان لها على أبيها دين فجهزها أبوها ثم قال جهزتها بدينها علي وقالت بل بمالك فالقول للأب، وقيل للبنت. ولو دفع إلى أم ولده شيئاً الشيخ الإمام الذي اختاره في المحيط قوله (ليس له في الاستحسان أي ليس للأب قوله وقال قاضيخان وينبغي أن يكون الخ) قال في
In the fatwas of Qadi Khan: If a man brings cotton to his home and his wife spins it. If he says: 'Spin it for me', then it belongs to him and she has no wage. If he says: 'Spin it for us', then it belongs to him, and she has a wage like her. If he says: 'Spin it', then it belongs to him. And if he says: 'Spin it for yourself', then it belongs to her. If they dispute and she says: 'I said spin it for yourself', and he denies her, then the statement is his with his oath. If he forbids her from spinning it and she spins it, then it belongs to her because she is a usurper, and he has a like of his cotton from her. If they dispute over the prohibition, then the statement is his. If he did not forbid nor permit, and she spun it, then if he is a cotton seller, it belongs to her and she must give a like of his cotton; otherwise, it belongs to him. Up to the end of what is in the fatwas. Here are sub-issues mentioned in the fatwas; it is good to mention them as they are important. First: If a man seeks marriage with a woman in her brother's house, and the brother refuses to give her except in exchange for money given to him, so he gives it, then marries her, the husband may take back what he gave. Second: If a man seeks marriage with a man's daughter, and her father says: 'If you pay such-and-such as dower, I will marry her to you', then after that he sends gifts to the father's house, but he cannot pay the dower and does not marry her, and he wants to take back the gifts, they said: what he sent for the dower, whether in existence or destroyed, he takes it back. Similarly, all that he sent as a gift while it is in existence; but as for destroyed or consumed, nothing is due. Third: If a man spends on another's woman in iddah hoping to marry her when her iddah ends, and when it ends she refuses. If he made a condition in the spending that she marry him, such as saying: 'I spend on condition that you marry me', he takes back the spending whether she marries him or not. Similarly, if he did not make a condition—according to the sound opinion he takes back; and it was said he does not take back if she marries herself, even if he had conditioned, and this was also declared sound. And if she refuses and he had not conditioned, he does not take back according to the sound opinion. Fourth: The issue of trousseau (jihaz). It contains two questions. First: Al-Mubtagha says: One whose wife is brought to him without a trousseau may demand from the father what he sent of dinars and dirhams. If the trousseau is insufficient, he may demand what is befitting for what he sent—meaning if she does not provide a trousseau befitting what he sent, he may take back what he sent. What is considered is what is prepared for the husband, not what is prepared for her. If he remains silent for a long time after the wedding, he may not litigate thereafter, even if nothing was prepared for him. If a father provides a trousseau for his daughter and hands it over to her, he may not, by istihsan, take it back from her, and fatwa is given accordingly. If the woman's family takes something upon delivery, the husband may take it back because it is bribery. Second: If a father provides a trousseau for his daughter, then claims that what he gave her was a loan ('ariyah), while she says it was a gift (tamlik); or the husband says so after her death to inherit from it, and the father says it was a loan, then in Fath al-Qadir, al-Tajnis, and al-Dhakhira, the chosen position for fatwa is that the statement is of the husband and her, if the custom is continuous that the father gives the like as a trousseau not as a loan, as in our lands. If custom is mixed, then the statement is of the father. Qadi Khan said: The answer should be detailed: if the father is from the nobles and generous, his claim that it is a loan is not accepted; if the father is from those who do not provide trousseau for daughters, his statement is accepted. The reality in our lands of Cairo is that custom is mixed, so fatwa is given that the statement is of the father. And if the statement is of the husband in the first case, and the father brings evidence, it is accepted. In al-Tajnis, al-Walwaljiah, and al-Dhakhira: The valid evidence is that he bear witness at the time of delivery to the woman that he handed over these items as a loan, or he writes a known copy and has the father witness her acknowledgement that all in this copy is the property of my father as a loan in my possession. But this suffices for the court, not for piety, because it is possible that he bought some of these items for her while she was a minor; with this acknowledgement the father does not become truthful in what is between him and Allah. The pious precaution is that he buys from her what is in this copy for a known price, then the daughter releases him from the price. Among the sub-issues of trousseau: If a father marries off his adult daughter and provides a trousseau of specific items but does not hand them over to her, then the marriage is annulled and he marries her to another, she cannot demand that trousseau from her father, because providing a trousseau is a gift, so delivery is required. If she had a debt on her father and he provides a trousseau, then says: I provided it as payment for her debt, and she says: Rather from your own wealth, the statement is of the father; and it was said for the daughter. If he gives something to his umm walad to prepare it as a trousseau for the daughter, and she does so and hands it over to her, her delivery is not valid. The minor issue. He said: It is the sound position, because he only spent for his intention, not on condition. End of the words of al-Fath. I say: What is understood from it is that the sound opinion is that he does not take back in any case when she marries him, whether he conditioned return or not, and he takes back when she refuses in any case. This is also what is understood from al-Imadiyya. What the author mentioned as the second opinion contradicts these, so let it be examined whence he took it. As for the first opinion he mentioned, it is in agreement with the absolute statement of al-Shaykh al-Imam, which he chose in al-Muhit. His statement: 'He does not have by istihsan' meaning that the father does not have. His statement: 'Qadi Khan said: The answer should be detailed...' He said in
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage from Bahr al-Ra'iq gathers several important rulings from fatwa collections on disputes arising from marriage-related financial transactions. The passage type is an expansion of rulings (expansion_probe). The author first presents the famous ruling from Qadi Khan on a wife spinning cotton brought by her husband. The key principle is that the husband's statement is taken over the wife's in disputes about the instruction, with his oath. The wife becomes a usurper if she spins against a prohibition, and the thread belongs to her but she must compensate for the cotton. Different scenarios are delineated based on the husband's explicit words. Then, several sub-issues (furu') are listed. The first concerns paying a brother to secure marriage: such payment is considered bribery (rishwa), and the husband may recover it. The second deals with gifts sent to the father as part of the dower: if the marriage does not occur, the gifts are recoverable if they still exist, but not if consumed. The third is a detailed case of spending on a woman in 'iddah hoping to marry her: the ruling hinges on whether a condition was made. According to the sound opinion, if the condition is explicit, the spender can recover his spending whether the woman marries him or not; if no condition was made, the rulings differ, but the relied-upon position (as per al-Fath and al-Imadiyya) is that he cannot recover if she marries him, but can recover if she refuses. The fourth issue concerns the bridal trousseau (jihaz). The first sub-issue gives the husband the right to demand a proper trousseau befitting what he sent, and if not provided, he may take back what he sent. However, if he remains silent for a long time after the wedding, he loses this right. The second sub-issue addresses the common dispute: is the father's delivery of the trousseau a gift or a loan? The ruling depends on custom (urf). In lands where the custom is for the father to give a gift, the husband's or wife's claim that it is a gift is accepted; if custom is mixed, the father's claim of a loan is believed. The author also discusses the role of evidence and the preferable form of proof to avoid sin. Finally, additional sub-rulings on trousseau are provided, such as when a father provides a trousseau before delivery and the marriage is annulled, or when the father claims the trousseau was in lieu of a debt. All these rules reflect the Hanafi school's reliance on custom, conditions, and the principle that in financial disputes the party claiming the default (bara'ah) or the one whose statement is supported by apparent circumstances is believed.

Key Terms
جهاز (jihāz): Trousseau: items (furniture, clothes, etc.) that the bride's family provides for the new household.
معتدة الغير (muʿtaddat al-ghayr): A woman observing the waiting period (ʿiddah) after divorce or death of another man (not her husband).
رشوة (rishwah): Bribe: any payment made to secure a marriage or any benefit that is unjust.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a region where custom is mixed (some fathers give gifts, some loans), a father provides a trousseau and claims it was a loan. The wife claims gift. Whose statement is accepted according to the chosen position?
A) The wife's, because she is in possession
B) The father's, because the custom is mixed
C) The husband's, if the wife is deceased
D) The statement is split evenly

Key Takeaway
Custom (urf) is the primary determinant in trousseau disputes; the husband's right to demand a proper trousseau is time-sensitive.

Husband's Right to Reclaim Gifts Upon Wife's Refusal
Ruling on husband's right to reclaim gifts when wife refuses marriage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three scenarios determining the husband's right to reclaim gifts under Hanafi law.

Core Idea
A husband may reclaim gifts if the wife refuses marriage, but not if she marries him or they have eaten together.

The relied-upon Hanafi position distinguishes three scenarios regarding gifts given before marriage. If the wife goes through with the marriage, the husband cannot reclaim them. If she refuses, he can—provided he actually gave them. However, if they have already shared a meal together, the right to reclaim is lost completely. This ruling, taken from al-Imadi's works, illustrates how Hanafi jurisprudence balances contractual expectations with relational conduct.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Three Scenarios
The text mentions three distinct situations: (1) the wife marries him, (2) she refuses, (3) she has eaten with him. List them.
2
Determine the Ruling for Each
For each scenario, state whether the husband has a right to reclaim (ruju') or not. If she marries him: no recourse. If she refuses: recourse (provided he gave the gift). If she ate with him: absolutely no recourse.
3
Note the Condition for Recourse
When she refuses, recourse is only possible if the husband had actually given the gift to her. This condition is explicit: 'if he had given it to her.'
4
Understand the Underlying Principle
The gift is implicitly conditional on the marriage. If the wife reneges, the condition fails, and the husband may reclaim. Eating together indicates informal acceptance or consummation, nullifying the right.
This is a form of istihsan based on customary practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أن المعتمد ما ذكره العمادي في فصوله أنها إن تزوجته لا رجوع أبت فله الرجوع إن كان دفع لها وإن أكلت معه فلا مطلقاً.
And the summary is that the relied-upon view is what al-Imadi mentioned in his chapters: that if she married him, there is no recourse; if she refuses, then he has recourse if he had given it to her; and if she ate with him, then absolutely no recourse.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This is a ruling statement from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq summarizing the relied-upon (mu'tamad) position regarding a husband's right to reclaim gifts or dower given before marriage. The ruling distinguishes three scenarios: if the woman goes through with the marriage, the husband cannot reclaim whatever he gave; if she refuses to marry, he can reclaim it provided he actually gave it to her; but if she had already eaten with him (i.e., they shared a meal, indicating informal acceptance), then he cannot reclaim at all. This is taken from al-Imadi's work 'Fusuluhu'.

Key Terms
رجوع (ruju'): The legal right of the husband to reclaim property (e.g., mahr or gifts) from the wife when the marriage is not consummated or the wife refuses.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the relied-upon position, when can a husband reclaim gifts given to his wife-to-be?
A) When the wife marries him
B) When the wife refuses and he had given her the gifts
C) When they have eaten together
D) When the wife refuses to eat with him

Key Takeaway
The husband's right to reclaim gifts depends on three scenarios: marriage (no recourse), refusal (recourse if gift given), and shared meal (no recourse absolutely).

The Husband's Right to Demand Jihaz
First sub-issue of jihaz: husband's rights and remedies

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two remedies available to a husband when his wife is brought without sufficient jihaz: demanding suitable jihaz from the father and recovering what he sent.

Core Idea
A husband can demand jihaz from the father if lacking; once delivered, the father cannot reclaim it by istihsan.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses the first sub-issue of jihaz: the husband's rights when the bride arrives without proper trousseau. The Hanafi school provides clear remedies based on custom and equity, balancing the husband's expectations with the father's goodwill.

Guided Path
1
Identify the husband's legal position
When the wife is brought without jihaz, the husband has a right to demand from the father an amount commensurate with what he sent (presumably as mahr or gifts). If the jihaz is insufficient, he can demand that it be made suitable.
The passage references al-Muntaba for this ruling.
2
State the first remedy: demand for suitable jihaz
If the jihaz is little, the husband may demand what is appropriate for what he sent. The measure is what is prepared for the husband, not for the wife.
Custom plays a role in determining suitability.
3
State the second remedy: recovery of what he sent
If no jihaz is provided at all, the husband can recover the dinars and dirhams he sent. This is an alternative remedy.
4
Note the istihsan rule: father cannot reclaim once delivered
If the father gives jihaz and delivers it to the daughter, by istihsan he cannot later reclaim it. The fatwa is upon this view.
This prevents the father from taking back what he gave.
5
Address the bribe ruling
If the woman's family took something at the time of delivery, the husband can reclaim it because it is considered a bribe (rishwah).
This protects the husband from extortion.

Source Text

الرابع مسألة الجهاز وفيه مسألتان : الأولى قال في المبتغى بالغين المعجمة : من زفت إليه امرأته بلا جهاز فله مطالبة الأب بما بعث إليه من الدنانير والدراهم، وإن كان الجهاز قليلاً فله المطالبة بما يليق بالمبعوث يعني إذا لم تجهر بما يليق بالمبعوث فله استرداد ما بعث، والمعتبر ما يتخذ للزوج لا ما يتخذ لها، ولو سكت بعد الزفاف طويلاً ليس له أن يخاصمه بعده وإن لم يتخذ له شيء. ولو جهز ابنته وسلمه إليها ليس له في الاستحسان استرداده منها وعليه الفتوى، ولو أخذ أهل المرأة شيئاً عند التسليم فللزوج أن يسترده لأنه رشوة. الثانية لو جهز بنته ثم ادعى أن ما دفعه لها عارية وقالت تمليكاً أو قال الزوج ذلك بعد موتها ليرث منه وقال الأب عارية، ففي فتح القدير والتجنيس والذخيرة المختار للفتوى أن القول للزوج ولها إذا كان العرف مستمراً أن الأب يدفع مثله جهازاً لا عارية كما في ديارنا ، وإن كان مشتركاً فالقول قول الأب. وقال قاضيخان : وينبغي أن يكون الجواب على التفصيل إن كان الأب من الأشراف والكرام لا يقبل قوله أنه عارية، وإن كان الأب ممن لا يجهز البنات بمثل ذلك قبل قوله اهـ. والواقع في ديارنا القاهرة أن العرف مشترك فيفتى بأن القول للأب وإذا كان القول للزوج في الأولى فأقام الأب بينة قبلت قال في التجنيس والولوالجية والذخيرة: والبينة الصحيحة أن يشهد عند التسليم إلى المرأة أني إنما سلمت هذه الأشياء بطريق العارية أو يكتب نسخة معلومة ويشهد الأب على إقرارها أن جميع ما في هذه النسخة ملك والدي عارية في يدي منه لكن هذا يصلح للقضاء لا للاحتياط لجواز أنه اشترى لها بعض هذه الأشياء في حالة الصغر فبهذا الإقرار لا يصير الأب صادقاً فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى والاحتياط أن يشتري منها ما في هذه النسخة بثمن معلوم ثم إن البنت تبرئه عن الثمن .اهـ.
The fourth issue: the matter of jihaz. It contains two sub-issues: The first: It is said in al-Muntaba (with a dotted ghain): The one whose wife is brought to him without jihaz has the right to demand from the father what was sent to him of dinars and dirhams. If the jihaz is little, he has the right to demand what is suitable for what was sent, meaning if she did not bring out what is suitable for what was sent, he has the right to recover what he sent. What is considered is what is prepared for the husband, not what is prepared for her. If he remains silent after the wedding night for a long time, he has no right to dispute with him afterward, even if nothing was prepared for him. If he gave his daughter jihaz and delivered it to her, he has no right by istihsan to reclaim it from her, and upon this is the fatwa. If the woman's family took something at the time of delivery, the husband has the right to reclaim it, because it is a bribe. The second: If he gave his daughter jihaz, then claimed that what he gave her was a loan (ariyah), and she said it was a gift (tamlik), or the husband said that after her death to inherit from him, and the father said it was a loan, then in Fath al-Qadir, al-Tajnis, and al-Dhakhira, the chosen position for fatwa is that the word of the husband and her (the daughter) is accepted if the custom is continuous that the father gives the like as jihaz, not as a loan, as in our lands. If it is mixed, then the father's word is accepted. Qadi Khan said: The answer should be detailed: if the father is from the nobility and generous people, his claim that it is a loan is not accepted; if the father is from those who do not give their daughters jihaz of that amount, his word is accepted. The reality in our lands, Cairo, is that the custom is mixed, so the fatwa is that the father's word is accepted. If the husband's word is accepted in the first case, and the father brings evidence, it is accepted. It is said in al-Tajnis, al-Walwaljiyya, and al-Dhakhira: The correct evidence is that he should testify at the time of delivery to the woman that 'I only delivered these things as a loan', or he writes a known copy and has the father witness her acknowledgment that all that is in this copy is my father's property as a loan in my possession. But this is suitable for judicial judgment, not for precaution, because it is possible that he bought some of these things for her while she was a minor, so by this acknowledgment the father does not become truthful between himself and Allah. The precaution is that he buys from her what is in this copy for a known price, then the daughter discharges him from the price.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the rulings concerning jihaz (the trousseau or dowry provided by the bride's family). It presents two main sub-issues. The first sub-issue addresses the husband's rights when the wife is brought without a sufficient jihaz: he can demand from the father an amount commensurate with what the husband sent (presumably as a gift or mahr). If the father later delivers the jihaz, by istihsan he cannot reclaim it. The second sub-issue deals with disputes over ownership: whether the jihaz is a gift (tamlik) or a loan (ariyah). The relied-upon position depends on custom: if it is customary for the father to give such items as jihaz, the word of the husband or daughter is taken; if custom is mixed, the father's word is taken unless he is from a noble family. The passage also provides a method for proper evidence to avoid future disputes, including a precautionary practice of buying the items from the daughter.

Key Terms
جهاز (jihāz): The trousseau or dowry provided by the bride's family to the groom for the household.
عارية (ʿāriyah): A loan given for temporary use without the expectation of return of the same item.
تمليك (tamlīk): Transfer of ownership; a gift that permanently transfers property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He can still demand jihaz at any time.
B) He loses his right to dispute.
C) He can only recover half of what he sent.
D) The father must still provide jihaz.

Key Takeaway
A husband can demand jihaz from the father if lacking; once jihaz is delivered, the father cannot reclaim it by istihsan, and any extra payment taken at delivery is a bribe and recoverable.

Disputes over Ownership of Jihaz: Gift or Loan?
Second sub-issue of jihaz: ownership disputes (ariyah vs tamlik)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for determining ownership of jihaz when the father claims it was a loan (ariyah) and the daughter/husband claims it was a gift (tamlik).

Core Idea
In jihaz ownership disputes, custom decides: if custom is for fathers to give jihaz as a gift, the father's claim of loan is rejected; if custom is mixed, the father's word is accepted.

The second sub-issue of jihaz addresses a common family dispute: after the father provides jihaz, he may later claim it was only a loan, while the daughter or her husband claim it was a gift. The Hanafi school relies heavily on custom (urf) and the social status of the father to resolve this. The passage also provides a prudent practice for proper evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
The father claims the jihaz was a loan (ariyah) for temporary use. The daughter or husband claim it was a gift (tamlik) that transferred ownership.
Further, the husband may claim after the daughter's death that he inherits it as her property.
2
Apply the default rule based on custom
If the custom (urf) is continuous that fathers give such items as jihaz (gift), then the father's claim of ariyah is not accepted. The word of the husband/daughter is taken.
This is the chosen position in Fath al-Qadir, al-Tajnis, and al-Dhakhira.
3
Apply the rule when custom is mixed
If the custom is mixed (some fathers give as gift, some as loan), then the father's word is accepted. This is the fatwa in Cairo according to the author.
4
Consider Qadi Khan's refinement
Qadi Khan adds detail: if the father is from the noble and generous class, his claim of ariyah is rejected; if he is from those who do not give jihaz of that amount, his word is accepted.
This refinement considers social status as an indicator of custom.
5
Understand the proper evidence procedure
To preempt disputes, the father should testify at the time of delivery that it is a loan, or have the daughter sign an acknowledgment. However, this is suitable only for judicial judgment, not for precaution before Allah. The precaution is to buy the items from the daughter for a known price and have her discharge him.
This shows the meticulousness of the Hanafi jurists in protecting both parties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابع مسألة الجهاز وفيه مسألتان : الأولى قال في المبتغى بالغين المعجمة : من زفت إليه امرأته بلا جهاز فله مطالبة الأب بما بعث إليه من الدنانير والدراهم، وإن كان الجهاز قليلاً فله المطالبة بما يليق بالمبعوث يعني إذا لم تجهر بما يليق بالمبعوث فله استرداد ما بعث، والمعتبر ما يتخذ للزوج لا ما يتخذ لها، ولو سكت بعد الزفاف طويلاً ليس له أن يخاصمه بعده وإن لم يتخذ له شيء. ولو جهز ابنته وسلمه إليها ليس له في الاستحسان استرداده منها وعليه الفتوى، ولو أخذ أهل المرأة شيئاً عند التسليم فللزوج أن يسترده لأنه رشوة. الثانية لو جهز بنته ثم ادعى أن ما دفعه لها عارية وقالت تمليكاً أو قال الزوج ذلك بعد موتها ليرث منه وقال الأب عارية، ففي فتح القدير والتجنيس والذخيرة المختار للفتوى أن القول للزوج ولها إذا كان العرف مستمراً أن الأب يدفع مثله جهازاً لا عارية كما في ديارنا ، وإن كان مشتركاً فالقول قول الأب. وقال قاضيخان : وينبغي أن يكون الجواب على التفصيل إن كان الأب من الأشراف والكرام لا يقبل قوله أنه عارية، وإن كان الأب ممن لا يجهز البنات بمثل ذلك قبل قوله اهـ. والواقع في ديارنا القاهرة أن العرف مشترك فيفتى بأن القول للأب وإذا كان القول للزوج في الأولى فأقام الأب بينة قبلت قال في التجنيس والولوالجية والذخيرة: والبينة الصحيحة أن يشهد عند التسليم إلى المرأة أني إنما سلمت هذه الأشياء بطريق العارية أو يكتب نسخة معلومة ويشهد الأب على إقرارها أن جميع ما في هذه النسخة ملك والدي عارية في يدي منه لكن هذا يصلح للقضاء لا للاحتياط لجواز أنه اشترى لها بعض هذه الأشياء في حالة الصغر فبهذا الإقرار لا يصير الأب صادقاً فيما بينه وبين الله تعالى والاحتياط أن يشتري منها ما في هذه النسخة بثمن معلوم ثم إن البنت تبرئه عن الثمن .اهـ.
The fourth issue: the matter of jihaz. It contains two sub-issues: The first: It is said in al-Muntaba (with a dotted ghain): The one whose wife is brought to him without jihaz has the right to demand from the father what was sent to him of dinars and dirhams. If the jihaz is little, he has the right to demand what is suitable for what was sent, meaning if she did not bring out what is suitable for what was sent, he has the right to recover what he sent. What is considered is what is prepared for the husband, not what is prepared for her. If he remains silent after the wedding night for a long time, he has no right to dispute with him afterward, even if nothing was prepared for him. If he gave his daughter jihaz and delivered it to her, he has no right by istihsan to reclaim it from her, and upon this is the fatwa. If the woman's family took something at the time of delivery, the husband has the right to reclaim it, because it is a bribe. The second: If he gave his daughter jihaz, then claimed that what he gave her was a loan (ariyah), and she said it was a gift (tamlik), or the husband said that after her death to inherit from him, and the father said it was a loan, then in Fath al-Qadir, al-Tajnis, and al-Dhakhira, the chosen position for fatwa is that the word of the husband and her (the daughter) is accepted if the custom is continuous that the father gives the like as jihaz, not as a loan, as in our lands. If it is mixed, then the father's word is accepted. If the husband's word is accepted in the first case, and the father brings evidence, it is accepted. The precaution is that he buys from her what is in this copy for a known price, then the daughter discharges him from the price.

Conditions of Delivery in Jihaz and Resulting Disputes
Branch rulings on jihaz delivery and ownership

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle that jihaz is a gift (tamlik) requiring delivery to resolve disputes over ownership and post-death claims.

Core Idea
Delivery of jihaz is necessary for ownership; without it, the daughter cannot claim it after remarriage.

This lesson examines several branch cases from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq that illustrate the principle that jihaz is a gift requiring delivery. These cases cover scenarios from missing delivery to parental silence, all revolving around who owns the jihaz items and what happens after the parents' death.

Guided Path
1
Recognize jihaz as a gift
Identify that providing jihaz is legally considered a gift (tamlik), not a loan or obligatory payment. This classification triggers specific rules for validity and ownership.
This is the key premise underlying all branches.
2
Understand the delivery condition
For a gift to be validly transferred, delivery (taslim) is required. Without delivery, the gift is not complete and the donee has no right to demand it later if circumstances change.
Hanafi law: qabd (taking possession) is essential for hibah.
3
Apply to each branch case
Work through each case (nondelivery after annulment, debt dispute, umm walad delivery, weaving, silence, customary spending) and determine ownership based on whether delivery occurred or other factors (e.g., custom, presence).
Pay attention to the rulings: father's word accepted over daughter's, umm walad's delivery invalid for minor, father's silence prevents reclaim.
4
Note the open question
The passage ends with a question about whether the same rules apply to the grandfather and mother. This shows areas of ikhtilaf and the need for ijtihad.
This case is left for fatwa; no clear transmission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن فروع الجهاز لو زوج ابنته البالغة وجهزها بأمتعة معينة ولم يسلمها إليها ثم فسخ العقد وزوجها من آخر فليس لها مطالبة الأب بذلك الجهاز لأن التجهيز تمليك فيشترط فيه التسليم، ولو كان لها على أبيها دين فجهزها أبوها ثم قال جهزتها بدينها علي وقالت بل بمالك فالقول للأب، وقيل للبنت. ولو دفع إلى أم ولده شيئاً لتتخذه جهازاً للبنت ففعلت وسلمته إليها لا يصح تسليمها. صغيرة نسجت جهازاً بمال أمها وأبيها وسعيها حال صغرها وكبرها فماتت أمها فسلم أبوها جميع الجهاز إليها فليس لإخوتها دعوى نصيبهم من جهة الأم امرأة نسجت في بيت أبيها شيئاً كثيراً من إبر يسم كان يشتريه أبوها ثم مات الأب فهذه الأشياء لها باعتبار العادة، ولو دفعت في تجهيزها لبنتها أشياء من أمتعة الأب بحضرته وعلمه وكان ساكتاً وزفت إليه أي إلى الزوج فليس للأب أن يسترد ذلك من بنته، وكذا لو انفقت الأم في جهازها ما هو معتاد والأب ساكت لا تضمن الكل في القنية في باب تجهيز البنات وبهذا يعلم أن الأب أو الأم إذا جهز بنته ثم مات فليس لبقية الورثة على الجهاز سبيل لكن هل هذا الحكم المذكور في الأب يتأتى في الأم والجد، فلو جهزها جدها ثم ماتت وقال ملكي وقال زوجها ملكها صارت واقعة الفتوى ولم أر فيها نقلاً صريحاً.
And from the branches of jihaz: If he married off his adult daughter and gave her jihaz consisting of specific items but did not deliver them to her, then he annulled the marriage and married her to another, she has no right to demand that jihaz from the father, because the provision of jihaz is a gift (tamlik) that requires delivery. If she had a debt against her father, and he gave her jihaz and then said 'I gave her the jihaz as payment of the debt she owes me,' and she said 'Rather with your own money,' then the father's word is accepted; it is also said the daughter's word is accepted. If he gave something to the mother of his child (umm walad) to make jihaz for the daughter, and she did so and delivered it to her, her delivery is not valid because she is a minor. A woman wove jihaz from the wealth of her mother and father and her own effort during her minority and majority, then her mother died, and her father gave all the jihaz to her; then her siblings have no claim to their share from the mother's side. A woman wove many things in her father's house from silk thread that her father used to buy, then her father died; these things belong to her by custom. If she used some of the father's goods in equipping her daughter with his presence and knowledge while he remained silent, and she was married off to the husband, then the father cannot reclaim that from his daughter. Similarly, if the mother spent on her jihaz what is customary while the father was silent, she is not liable for the whole, as in al-Qunyah in the chapter on equipping daughters. By this it is known that if the father or mother gave jihaz to their daughter and then died, the other heirs have no way to claim that jihaz. But does this ruling mentioned about the father apply to the mother and grandfather? For if the grandfather gave jihaz to his granddaughter, then she died, and he said 'My property' and her husband said 'Her property', it becomes a case for fatwa, and I have not seen a clear transmission about it.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq continues the discussion on jihaz with several branch cases. Key points: (1) Delivery (taslim) is a condition for valid jihaz; if not delivered, the daughter cannot demand it after remarriage. (2) If a daughter had a debt, and the father claims the jihaz was repoayment, his word is accepted. (3) A mother of the child (umm walad) cannot validly deliver jihaz on behalf of a minor daughter. (4) A woman who weaved jihaz with her own effort and her parents' money owns it; siblings have no claim to the mother's share. (5) If the mother uses the father's goods with his silent knowledge, he cannot reclaim. (6) Ultimately, jihaz given by parents is not part of the inheritance for other heirs. There is a question whether the same rule applies to the mother and grandfather; the author expresses uncertainty.

Key Terms
تسليم (taslīm): Delivery or transfer of possession, a condition for the validity of gifting jihaz.
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who has borne a child to her master, with special legal status.
جهاز (jihāz): The household items or trousseau given by a bride's family to equip the new home; legally a gift.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling if a daughter receives jihaz from her father but then he claims it was repayment for a debt she owed him?
A) The daughter's word is accepted unconditionally.
B) The father's word is accepted (the preferred view).
C) The property is split equally between them.
D) The jihaz is invalidated.

Key Takeaway
Jihaz is a gift requiring delivery; without delivery, ownership remains with the giver, and the rules of inheritance do not apply until the gift is completed.
